Types of Unity of Motion and Plato's Objection
Author discusses types of unity of motion and Plato's denial of motion having unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of unity (by genus, species, person) in a given motion example and explain Plato's objection.

Core Idea
Motion can be one by genus, species, or person, but some deny its unity entirely.

In this passage, the author clarifies that motion can be considered one in three distinct ways: by genus (e.g., all motions are changes), by species (e.g., all motions of a specific kind like locomotion), or by person (e.g., a particular instance). He then reports an objection from Platonic thinkers who deny any unity to motion because it is not a stable thing and is divided by time. Understanding these classifications helps us avoid confusion when discussing motion in logical and theological contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of unity
The author states motion may be one by genus, by species, or by person. Note how each type represents a different level of abstraction: genus (general kind), species (specific kind), person (individual instance).
These are the same three types of unity used for any category.
2
Understand Plato's objection
Some followers of Plato absolutely deny that motion can be described as having unity or a state. Their reason: motion has no subsistence (it is not a stable entity) and it is divided according to time, implying that it cannot be one.
This objection challenges whether motion can be considered 'one' in any sense.
3
Reflect on the scope of logic
The author dismisses a full investigation into motion as beyond logic's domain, mentioning only what is needed. Recognize that logic does not solve all philosophical problems; it provides tools for reasoning.
This illustrates the boundaries of the discipline of logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الحركة قد تكون واحدة بالجنس وقد تكون واحدة بالنوع وقد تكون واحدة بالشخص، وقوم من أصحاب أفلاطون [Platon] منعوا كل المنع أن تكون الحركة توصف بالهيئة أو بالوحدة لما أنه لا حصول لشيء منها، وأنها منقسمة حسب انقسام الزمان، وقد كان من اللوازم أن يكون زمانها واحدًا. وبالجملة فالبحث في أحوال الحركة قصة في شرحها طول؛ وذلك بمعزل عن هذه الصناعة فلا يليق إدخاله فيها إلا بقدر الحاجة، وذلك هو الذي مرَّ ذكره.
Then motion may be one by genus, may be one by species, and may be one by person. And a group of the followers of Plato [Platon] absolutely denied that motion can be described as having a state or unity, because no part of it has subsistence, and that it is divided according to the division of time, and it would be necessary that its time be one. In summary, the investigation into the states of motion is a long story in its explanation; and that is remote from this craft, so it is not appropriate to introduce it except to the extent of need, and that is what has been mentioned.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a brief supplementary note on the unity of motion. The author explains that motion can be one in three ways: by genus (e.g., all motions are changes), by species (e.g., all motions of increase), or by individual (e.g., this specific motion of Zayd walking). He then reports an objection from some followers of Plato who completely deny that motion can be described as having any unity or state, because motion is not a stable thing and is divided along with time. The author dismisses a full investigation as beyond the scope of logic, indicating that only as much as needed has been mentioned.

Key Terms
حركة
بالجنس
بالنوع
بالشخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did some followers of Plato deny unity to motion?
A) Because motion is not a substance
B) Because motion has no subsistence and is divided by time
C) Because motion is only one by genus
D) Because motion is an accident

Key Takeaway
The unity of motion can be understood at three levels, but Platonic thinkers argue it has no real unity due to its temporal division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Consider the motion of a specific object in an Islamic theological context, e.g., the annual motion of the Earth around the Sun (if held). Classify it as one by genus, species, or person according to the author's three types.
?
2. Explain Plato's objection in your own words and evaluate whether it would apply to the motion you classified. Does the objection undermine the possibility of treating that motion as one?

Definition and Division of Opposites
Author defines opposites and announces their division into four kinds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define two opposites (mutaqābilān) in terms of the impossibility of coexistence and identify the basis for their division into four types.

Core Idea
Opposites are defined by mutual exclusion in one subject at one time — not by mere difference.

In this chapter, the author introduces the concept of opposites (al-mutaqābilāt), a fundamental notion in logic that underpins many arguments, especially in kalām and uṣūl al-fiqh. Understanding the precise definition of opposites is crucial before exploring their classification. The passage gives the definition and announces a division into four kinds, which will be elaborated in subsequent sections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
What is being defined? The term 'two opposites' (al-mutaqābilān). Note that it is a relational term: opposites exist in pairs.
2
Extract the essential condition
The defining characteristic is the impossibility of coexistence (imtināʿ al-ijtimāʿ) in one subject (maḥall) at one time (zamān). This is the core of opposition.
The text literally says 'they come together' but logically it means 'cannot come together'.
3
Apply to an example
Consider the propositions 'This is ḥalāl' and 'This is ḥarām'. They cannot both be true of the same action at the same time, so they are opposites.
4
Anticipate the division
The author states that opposites are of four divisions. This suggests that opposition can be based on different logical relationships, such as contradiction, contrariety, privation, or correlation.
The text cuts off but the announcement sets up the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في المتقابلات. أما المتقابلان فهما اللذان يجتمعان في محل واحد في زمان واحد معا، وإنه على أربعة أقسام؛ وذلك لأن التقابل بين الشيئين وهو امتناع الاجتماع بينهما في محل واحد في زمان واحد لا يخلو من أن يكون بين الوجودين؛ وإنه
Chapter on opposites. As for the two opposites, they are the two that come together in one subject at one time together, and they are of four divisions; because the opposition between two things is the impossibility of their coexistence in one subject at one time; it is inevitable that it be between two existences; and it...
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter on opposites (al-mutaqabilat). The author defines two opposites as things that cannot coexist in the same subject at the same time. This is the standard logical definition. He then states that opposites are of four divisions, implying a classification that will follow (likely the four types: contradictory, privative, contrary, and correlative). The text cuts off but the definition is clear. The study of opposites is fundamental in logic for understanding propositions and arguments, such as the contradiction between 'everything is created' and 'something is not created'.

Key Terms

تقابل
امتناع الاجتماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are different in name
B) They cannot coexist in one subject at one time
C) They are always in different places
D) They are mutually dependent

Key Takeaway
Two opposites are defined by the impossibility of coexistence in one subject at one time; opposition is of four kinds, determined by whether the opposition is between two existences or other combinations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Consider the two statements: 'It is obligatory to pray' and 'It is forbidden to pray'. Are they opposites? Identify the subject and time condition.

Motion Occurs in Four Categories
Author restricts motion to four categories and defines change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four categories in which motion occurs and distinguish primary from accidental motion.

Core Idea
Motion is not a category but occurs in four: quality, quantity, place, and position.

This passage defines motion as gradual change and specifies which categories it can belong to. Understanding this classification is essential for later discussions on change in theology and philosophy, where motion is often evidence for contingency.

Guided Path
1
Find the definition of motion
Look for phrases describing motion as gradual acquisition of a disposition or form. The text uses 'tadarruj' (gradualness).
The definition is implicit: motion is a change that occurs little by little.
2
List the four categories
Identify the names: كيف (quality), كم (quantity), أين (place), وضع (position).
These are the only categories where motion occurs primarily.
3
Understand accidental motion
The text says motion in other categories (substance, relation, etc.) is only accidental if it involves one of the four first.
For example, change in substance (like creation) is not motion per se.
4
Recognize the logical purpose
The classification is needed to define motion in logic, not to fully explore natural philosophy.
The author later states that a full discussion belongs elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في اكتساب الهيئة والصورة التي بها يصح أن يصدر الفعل؛ وذلك على سبيل التدرج فيكون أن يفعل غاية لذلك التدرج. ثم التبدل الذي يتقدمه بوجوه مختلفة منها أن يكون بالطبع ومنها أن يكون بالإرادة؛ وذلك قد يكون بالآلة وقد لا يكون. فإذن التبدل إما في القوة، وإما في العزيمة، وإما في الآلة وبعد ذلك في الفعل بالعرض. وعلى هذا في أن ينفعل فإنه غاية لذلك التدرج أيضًا. ولا يمتنع أن يكون الشيء قد يتغير من أن لا يكون ينفعل بالحد إلى أن ينفعل بالحد ويكون ذلك يسيرًا يسيرًا فيظن أنه حركة.؛ فيعلم من هذه الجملة أن الحركة إنما تقع في المقولات الأربع وهي الكيف والكم والأين والوضع كما مرَّ، وفي الباقي منها لا تكون إلا وأن تكون في واحد منها أولا؛ وذلك بالعرض على ما عرف. ثم الحركة قد تكون واحدة بالجنس وقد تكون واحدة بالنوع وقد تكون واحدة بالشخص، وقوم من أصحاب أفلاطون [Platon] منعوا كل المنع أن تكون الحركة توصف بالهيئة أو بالوحدة لما أنه لا حصول لشيء منها، وأنها منقسمة حسب انقسام الزمان، وقد كان من اللوازم أن يكون زمانها واحدًا. وبالجملة فالبحث في أحوال الحركة قصة في شرحها طول؛ وذلك بمعزل عن هذه الصناعة فلا يليق إدخاله فيها إلا بقدر الحاجة، وذلك هو الذي مرَّ ذكره.
In acquiring the disposition and form by which the action can proceed; and that by way of gradualness, so that it becomes an act that is an end of that gradualness. Then the change that precedes it in various ways: sometimes by nature, sometimes by will; and that may be with an instrument or without. So change is either in potentiality, or in determination, or in the instrument, and after that in the accidental act. And likewise in being affected, it is also an end of that gradualness. And it is not impossible that a thing changes from not being affected by the limit to being affected by the limit, and that occurs little by little, so it is thought to be motion. From this summary it is known that motion occurs only in the four categories: quality, quantity, place, and position, as has passed, and in the rest it does not occur except that it is in one of them first; that is accidentally, as known. Then motion may be one in genus, one in species, and one in individual. And a group of Plato's followers absolutely denied that motion can be described by form or unity, because nothing of it subsists, and that it is divided according to the division of time, and it would be necessary that its time be one. In short, the investigation of the states of motion is a lengthy story; that is far from this discipline, so it is not appropriate to introduce it except to the extent of need, and that is what has been mentioned.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion on motion. It first defines motion as the gradual acquisition of a disposition or form. Change (tabaddul) is divided into types: natural or voluntary, with or without an instrument. Change occurs in potentiality, determination, or instrument, then accidentally in action. The key point: motion occurs only in four categories—quality (kayf), quantity (kam), place (ayn), and position (wada'). In other categories (substance, relation, etc.), motion is only accidental if it involves one of these four. Motion is then classified by unity: by genus (e.g., any change of place), by species (e.g., walking), or by individual (this specific walk). The passage then refutes Platonists who deny motion has unity or form because motion is divisible by time. The author responds that this objection would require motion's time to be one, which is not necessary. Finally, he notes that a full discussion of motion belongs to natural philosophy, not logic, so only the relevant part was presented.

Key Terms
حركة
كيف
كم
أين
وضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Two
B) Four
C) Six
D) All ten

Key Takeaway
Motion is not a separate category but occurs primarily in quality, quantity, place, and position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
List the four categories of motion mentioned in the passage. For each category, provide a concrete example from the natural world (e.g., a leaf changing colour is motion in quality).
?
Explain why motion in the category of substance is impossible according to this passage. Use the text's claim that motion occurs only in these four categories primarily.

Unity of Motion and the Platonist Objection
Author classifies unity of motion and refutes Platonist denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of unity of motion (genus, species, individual) and summarize the Platonist objection and the author's response.

Core Idea
Motion can be one by genus, species, or individual; the Platonist denial of unity fails because time's division doesn't undermine it.

Having established where motion occurs, the author now discusses how motion can be considered one. He distinguishes three levels of unity and then addresses an objection from Platonists who claim motion cannot have unity because it is divisible over time. The response is brief but sufficient for the logical context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of unity
Find the phrases 'واحدة بالجنس', 'واحدة بالنوع', 'واحدة بالشخص'. Understand each: genus (e.g., any local motion), species (e.g., walking), individual (e.g., a specific walk).
These divisions are logical, not physical.
2
Understand the Platonist objection
Plato's followers deny that motion can have form or unity because motion does not subsist and is divisible by time.
They argue that if motion were one, its time would have to be one, but time is divisible.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author implies that the objection's premise (time must be one) is not necessary; the full discussion is beyond logic.
He dismisses further debate as belonging to natural philosophy.
4
Recognize the boundary
The author states that the investigation of motion's states is lengthy and not appropriate to logic except as needed.
This shows the self-limitation of the logical discipline.

Classification of Opposition in Islamic Logic
Author divides opposition into mutual correlation, contrariety, privation, and negation/affirmation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four types of opposition (taqābīl) discussed in the passage: correlation, contrariety, possession/privation, and negation/affirmation.

Core Idea
Opposition is broader than contradiction; it includes correlation, contrariety, privation, and simple negation.

This passage from a classical logic textbook presents a systematic classification of opposition. The author distinguishes four main types, each with unique characteristics. Understanding these types is essential for precise reasoning in Islamic sciences, especially when analyzing definitions and arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main division
The author begins by dividing opposition based on whether the two relata are conceptualized in relation to another or not. Note which types belong to each branch.
Correlatives (al-mutadha‘ifān) are relational; contraries (al-mutadāddān) are not.
2
Extract the first two types
Correlatives (e.g., owner-owned) and contraries (e.g., black-white) are the first division. Observe that correlatives do not require a receptive subject.
3
Identify the second division
The author further divides opposition based on the presence or absence of a receptive subject. This yields possession/privation (with subject) and negation/affirmation (without subject reference).
Possession/privation examples: movement/stillness.
4
Distinguish simple vs composite negation
Within negation/affirmation, note the difference between simple (e.g., horse vs non-horse) and composite (e.g., 'Zaid is a horse' vs 'Zaid is not a horse'). Simple has no truth value; composite does.
5
Understand the property of simple negation
Simple negation does not prevent coexistence in a subject (e.g., smell is not taste, but both can be in an apple). Other types like contraries prevent coexistence.
Contrast with correlatives and contraries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على قسمين: فإن كل واحد منهما من حيث هو لا يخلو من أن يكون معقولاً بالقياس إلى الغير وهما المتضايفان كالمالك والمملوك مثلا؛ وهذا من جملة ما سبق ذكره في المقولات، أو لا يكون وهما المتضادان كالسواد والبياض كذلك. أو بين العدمين؛ وذلك لا يمكن فإن من المحال أن يكون العدم متميزا عن العدم، فضلا عن أن يكون مقابلا له ومنافيًا إياه فيكون بين المختلفين لا محالة. وذلك على قسمين أيضًا: فإن كل واحد منهما لا يخلو من أن يكون مجرَّدًا عن تحقق الموضوع المستعد لقبوله بحسب الجنس أو النوع أو الشخص وهما المتضايفان : وهذا من جملة ما يجيء من بعد، أو لا يكون بل يعتبر ذلك في الكل؛ وهما الملكة والعدم الحقيقيتان كالحركة والسكون لما كان السكون عبارة عن عدم الحركة فيما من شأنه أن يتحرك؛ إذ المشهوران فإنهما باشتراط الموضوع القابل بل بحسب الوقت كما في القدرة على الشيء عند المشية وارتفاعها عن المادة المتهيئة للقبول. ولما كان التقابل منقسما إلى هذه الأقسام كان بالضرورة أعم من السلب والإيجاب، وكذلك السلب والإيجاب من السلب والإيجاب في المتناقضين وغيره. ثم التقابل بين السلب والإيجاب إما أن يكون بسيطًا كتقابل الفرس واللا فرس، وإما أن يكون مركبا كما في قولنا زيد فرس، زيد ليس بفرس»: والأول مما لا صدق فيه ولا كذب بخلاف الثاني؛ فإن فيه صدق وكذب. ويشتركان في أنه ليس فيهما إشارة إلى الوجود الخارجي فإن ذلك من قسم الملكة والعدم بل الاعتبار فيهما اعتبار أحكام عقلية فإنه إذا كانت اللا فرسية شيئًا له وجود بوجه كان السماء فيه (۱) سلوب موجودة بالفعل غير متناهية لا مرة واحدة بل مرارًا غير متناهية. فإن كل جملة تعرض سلب مستأنف؛ وذلك محال فيكون التقابل فيهما في القول والضمير فقط . ومن خواص هذا القسم أنه لا يمنع اجتماع ما يقع عليه من المتقابلين في موضوع واحد بأن يكونا فيه لا بأن يكونا عليه لأن الرائحة مثلا ليست طعمًا؛ وتقابل الطعم من حيث ليس طعما، ويجتمعان في موضوع على سبيل الوجود فيه. فكل ما لم يجتمع على سبيل الوجود فيه فليس يجتمع في موضوع على سبيل القول عليه ولا ينعكس. وأما في الغير كما في الحركة والسكون والحرارة والبرودة والأبوة والبنوة والزوج والفرد مثلا فإنها من المتقابلات أيضًا لكن صُوَر هذه الأشياء متخالفة؛ فإن الفرس جوهر دون اللَّا فرس والفرس واللا فرس ليس تقابلهما تقابل النقيضين؛ إذ لا صدق هناك ولا كذب ولا تقابل المتضايفين أيضًا، ولا تقابل الضدين كذلك لما مرَّ في شأن التضايف والتضاد. وأما الزوج والفرد فليس لهما موضوع واحد يتعاقبان عليه بل جنس واحد الموضوعين لهما لا يفارقانه. فموضوع الزوجية وهو العدد قد أخذ مع سلب الزوجية التي هي الانقسام بمتساويين؛ ووُضِع لذلك اسم وجودي هو الفرد. [۱] وعلى هذا في الحركة والسكون والذكورة والأنوثة والنور والظلمة / والخير والشر ونحو ذلك فإن الفرد كما يفهم أن الفردية معنى وجودي وليس كذلك فكذلك في جميع هذه الصور: فالظلمة عدم النور لا غير. وكذلك الشر عدم الخير، والسكون عدم الحركة هلم جرا وليس هذا الموضع موضع تحقيقه؛ إذ ذاك في صناعة أخرى. وإنما تعد هذه الأمور من الأضداد في هذا الموضع بناء على التعاريف عند السلف. فإن الجمهور منهم إما أن يعتقدوا أن هذه كلها أمور وجودية أو يعتقدوها أعدامًا ويعدوها من الأضداد لما أن الضدين عندهم هما اللذان لا يجتمعان في موضوع بل يتعاقبان وجودين كل واحد منهما أو لا وجودين. وأما في المتضايفين فليس بحسب التعاقب فيهما على
Upon two divisions: For each one of them in itself is not free from either being conceptual in relation to another — these are the two correlatives like owner and owned, for example; and this is among what was previously mentioned in the categories — or not being so, and these are the two contraries like black and white. Or between two non-existences; and that is impossible, for it is impossible for non-existence to be distinguished from non-existence, let alone to be opposite to it and contradictory to it. So it is necessarily between two different things. And that is also upon two divisions: for each one of them is not free from either being stripped of the realization of the subject ready to receive it according to genus, species, or individual — and these are the two correlatives: and this is among what will come later — or not, rather that is considered in both of them; and these are the genuine possession and privation like movement and stillness, since stillness is an expression of the absence of movement in what is apt to move; for the two famous ones are with the condition of the receptive subject, but according to time, as in ability to do a thing at will and its absence from the matter prepared to receive it. And since opposition is divided into these divisions, it is necessarily more general than negation and affirmation, and likewise negation and affirmation are from negation and affirmation in contradictories and others. Then the opposition between negation and affirmation is either simple, like the opposition of horse and non-horse, or composite, as in our saying 'Zaid is a horse, Zaid is not a horse': the first has neither truth nor falsehood unlike the second; for it has truth and falsehood. And they share that there is no reference to external existence in them, for that is from the division of possession and privation; rather the consideration in them is the consideration of intellectual judgments. For if non-horseness were a thing having existence in some respect, the sky would contain actually existent negations infinite not once but infinite times. For every sentence presents a new negation; and that is impossible, so opposition in them is only in speech and conscience. And among the properties of this division is that it does not prevent the union of the two opposites in one subject in the sense of being in it, not being predicated of it, because smell, for example, is not taste; and it opposes taste in that it is not taste, and they unite in a subject in the manner of existence in it. So everything that does not unite in the manner of existence in it does not unite in a subject in the manner of predication, and the converse does not hold. As for the other, like movement and stillness, heat and cold, fatherhood and sonship, and odd and even, for example, they are also among the opposites, but the forms of these things are diverse; for horse is a substance while non-horse is not, and the opposition of horse and non-horse is not the opposition of contradictories, since there is no truth or falsehood there, nor is it the opposition of correlatives, nor the opposition of contraries, due to what has passed concerning correlation and contrariety. As for odd and even, they do not have a single subject upon which they alternate, but rather a single genus of their two subjects that does not separate from them. For the subject of evenness, which is number, has been taken with the negation of evenness, which is equal division; and for that a positive name 'odd' was placed. And upon this [analogy] are movement and stillness, masculinity and femininity, light and darkness, good and evil, and the like. For the odd, as one understands that oddness is a positive meaning while it is not so, likewise in all these forms: darkness is the absence of light, nothing else; likewise evil is the absence of good, stillness is the absence of movement, and so on. And this is not the place for its investigation, for that is in another discipline. These things are only counted among the opposites here based on the definitions of the predecessors. For the majority of them either believe that all these are positive things or believe they are privations and count them among the opposites, because opposites according to them are two things that do not coexist in a subject but alternate, both being existent or both non-existent. As for the correlatives, they are not based on alternation in them.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of opposition (taqābīl). The author begins by dividing opposition into two main types based on whether the two relata are conceptualized in relation to another (mutadha'ifān, correlatives) or not (mutadāddān, contraries). He then notes that opposition cannot occur between two non-existences because non-existence cannot be distinguished from another non-existence; thus opposition is always between two distinct things. He further subdivides: either each relatum is devoid of a receptive subject (correlatives) or they require a subject (possession and privation, like movement and stillness, where stillness is the absence of movement in what is apt to move). This shows that opposition is broader than simple negation/affirmation (salb wa ījāb). Next, he distinguishes between simple opposition in negation/affirmation (e.g., 'horse' vs 'non-horse', which is neither true nor false) and composite opposition (e.g., 'Zaid is a horse' vs 'Zaid is not a horse', which has truth values). Both lack reference to external existence; that belongs to possession and privation. The author gives a proof: if non-horseness had existence, the sky would contain infinite actual negations, which is impossible; thus such opposition is only in speech and mind. A key property of the simple type is that it does not prevent the opposites from coexisting in a subject as attributes (e.g., smell is not taste, but both exist in the same apple). The author then contrasts this with other opposites like movement/stillness, heat/cold, etc., noting that horse/non-horse is not like contradictories, correlatives, or contraries. He discusses odd and even: they share a genus (number) but not a single subject; odd is the negation of evenness, yet given a positive name. He extends this to other examples like light/darkness, good/evil, which are privations according to the predecessors. The passage ends mid-sentence while discussing correlatives and alternation, indicating the discussion is incomplete.

المتضايفان
المتضادان
الملكة والعدم
السلب والإيجاب

A) Mutual correlation (al-mutadha‘ifān)
B) Contrariety (al-mutadāddān)
C) Privation and possession (al-malaka wa-l-‘adam)
D) Identity (al-huwiyya)

Key Takeaway
Opposition has four types: correlation, contrariety, possession/privation, and negation/affirmation, each with distinct logical properties.

The Four Types of Logical Opposition (Taqabul)
Author explains the four types of opposition with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of opposition (taqabul) and provide an Islamic science example for each.

Core Idea
Logical opposition is not only contradiction; it includes contrariety, privation/possession, and relation.

In this passage, the author presents a detailed classification of opposition that goes beyond simple contradiction. Understanding these types is crucial for analyzing definitions and arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, where terms like 'pure' and 'impure' or 'just' and 'unjust' involve different logical relations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus: opposition (taqabul)
The author treats taqabul as a genus that encompasses multiple species. In the text, he states: 'فالمتقابلات مما يقال على الأمور التي سبق ذكرها بمعنى أنها اشتراك في موضوع لها أن يوجد فيه' – opposition is a genus for its divisions as species.
2
Extract the four species from the passage
Look for the four types mentioned: contradiction (naqidayn), contrariety (diddayn), privation/possession ('adam wa mulk), and relation (mutadayifayn). The text explicitly names these and gives examples.
3
Distinguish each species by its defining feature
Contradictories involve truth and falsehood and cannot both be true or both false. Contraries are both positive and cannot coexist in a subject but can alternate. Privation/possession requires a subject naturally capable of the possession. Relatives are defined by mutual relation.
The text notes that some predecessors counted privations as contraries, but the logical distinction remains.
4
Test with examples from the text
For example, horse vs non-horse is not contradiction because no truth value; darkness vs light is privation/possession (darkness is absence of light); motion vs rest is often considered contrariety; fatherhood vs sonship is relation.
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
In fiqh, purity (tahara) and impurity (najasa) are privation and possession: a substance is pure by default, and impurity is the privation of that state. This classification helps in understanding rulings on ritual purity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقابل فيهما في القول والضمير فقط . ومن خواص هذا القسم أنه لا يمنع اجتماع ما يقع عليه من المتقابلين في موضوع واحد بأن يكونا فيه لا بأن يكونا عليه لأن الرائحة مثلا ليست طعمًا؛ وتقابل الطعم من حيث ليس طعما، ويجتمعان في موضوع على سبيل الوجود فيه. فكل ما لم يجتمع على سبيل الوجود فيه فليس يجتمع في موضوع على سبيل القول عليه ولا ينعكس. وأما في الغير كما في الحركة والسكون والحرارة والبرودة والأبوة والبنوة والزوج والفرد مثلا فإنها من المتقابلات أيضا لكن صور هذه الأشياء متخالفة؛ فإن الفرس جوهر دون اللافرس والفرس واللافرس ليس تقابلهما تقابل النقيضين؛ إذ لا صدق هناك ولا كذب ولا تقابل المتضايفين أيضا، ولا تقابل الضدين كذلك لما مر في شأن التضايف والتضاد.
وأما الزوج والفرد فليس لهما موضوع واحد يتعاقبان عليه بل جنس واحد الموضوعين لهما لا يفارقانه. فموضوع الزوجية وهو العدد قد أخذ مع سلب الزوجية التي هي الانقسام بمتساويين؛ ووضع لذلك اسم وجودي هو الفرد. [۱] وعلى هذا في الحركة والسكون والذكورة والأنوثة والنور والظلمة والخير والشر ونحو ذلك فإن الفرد كما يفهم أن الفردية معنى وجودي وليس كذلك فكذلك في جميع هذه الصور: فالظلمة عدم النور لا غير. وكذلك الشر عدم الخير، والسكون عدم الحركة هلم جرا وليس هذا الموضع موضع تحقيقه؛ إذ ذاك في صناعة أخرى. وإنما تعد هذه الأمور من الأضداد في هذا الموضع بناء على التعاريف عند السلف. فإن الجمهور منهم إما أن يعتقدوا أن هذه كلها أمور وجودية أو يعتقدوها أعداما ويعدوها من الأضداد لما أن الضدين عندهم هما اللذان لا يجتمعان في موضوع بل يتعاقبان وجودين كل واحد منهما أو لا وجودين. وأما في المتضايفين فليس بحسب التعاقب فيهما على موضوع أو اشتراكهما في موضوع حتى يكون الموضوع الذي هو علة لأمر ما يلزمه إمكان أن يصير فيه معلولا أو يكون هناك موضوع مشترك وإن كانت العلية والمعلولية من هذا الباب. وبالجملة فجميع الأشياء المتباينة الطبائع تكون متقابلة من حيث إن كل واحد منها ليس هو الآخر. وهذا هو التقابل الذي مر ذكره في الفرس واللافرس، وهو الذي يمنع اجتماع طرفيه على موضوع وإن لم يمنع ذلك في موضوع. ثم نقل التقابل عن اعتبار الحمل على الموضوع إلى اعتبار الموجود في الموضوع فجعل حال الأشياء تشترك في عام أو خاص تكون موجودة فيه بالقوة كالإيجاب والسلب اللذين موضوعهما المحمولات والموضوعات متعاقبة ولا تجتمع معًا.
وهذا بحكم القول، وليس في الوجود حمل ولا وضع وبعضه يكون من خارج. فمن ذلك ما تكون الشركة فيه في عام ومنها ما يكون في خاص ويكون المشترك فيه طبيعة هي بالقوة كلا الأمرين لكن لا يجتمعان فيه بل يتعاقبان عليه. فالمتقابلات مما يقال على الأمور التي سبق ذكرها بمعنى أنها اشتراك في موضوع لها أن يوجد فيه لا أنها تجتمع فيه فيكون معنى هذا التقابل كالجنس الأقسام له كالأنواع: وحينئذ يسهل على صاحب قاطيغورياس أن يقسم على الوجه الذي ينبغي فيقول: (المتقابل إما أن يكون ماهية مقولة بالقياس إلى ما هو مقابل له؛ وذلك هو التقابل بين المتضايفين كما مر من قبل، وإما أن لا يكون؛ وذلك إما أن يكون الموضوع صالحا للانتقال من أحد الطرفين بعينه إلى الآخر من غير انعكاس)، وهذا هو التقابل بين العدم والملكة فإنه إذا كان (مع الانعكاس فذلك يعد في قاطيغورياس من الأضداد كما مر). (۱) باري أرمينياس لأرسطو طاليس، ص۱۱۸ إلخ.
The opposition in them is only in predication and implication. And from the properties of this division is that it does not prevent the combination of what falls under the opposites in one subject inasmuch as they are in it, not inasmuch as they are predicated of it, because the smell, for example, is not taste; and the opposition of taste is inasmuch as it is not taste, and they combine in a subject in the manner of existence in it. So everything that does not combine in the manner of existence in it does not combine in a subject in the manner of predication of it, and the converse does not hold. As for the other, like motion and rest, heat and cold, fatherhood and sonship, even and odd, for example, they are also among the opposites, but the forms of these things are different. For the horse is a substance, unlike non-horse, and the opposition between horse and non-horse is not the opposition of contradictories, since there is no truth and falsehood there, nor is it the opposition of relatives, nor the opposition of contraries, as has passed in the discussion of relation and contrariety.
As for even and odd, they do not have a single subject that they alternate upon, but rather a single genus is the subject for both, not departing from them. So the subject of evenness, which is number, has been taken with the privation of evenness, which is division into two equal parts; and a positive name, 'odd', is assigned to that. [1] Similarly for motion and rest, masculinity and femininity, light and darkness, good and evil, and the like. For the individual, as it is understood that individuality is a positive notion, it is not so; likewise in all these forms: darkness is merely absence of light. Similarly, evil is absence of good, rest is absence of motion, and so on. But this is not the place to verify it, for that belongs to another science. Rather these matters are counted among contraries in this place based on the definitions of the predecessors. For the majority of them either believe that all these are positive matters, or they consider them privations and count them among contraries, because according to them the two contraries are those that do not combine in a subject but alternate, both being existent or non-existent. As for relatives, it is not according to alternation upon a subject or their sharing in a subject, such that the subject that is the cause of something entails the possibility of its becoming an effect in it, or there is a common subject, even if causality and effecthood are from this category. In summary, all things of different natures are opposite inasmuch as each one is not the other. This is the opposition that was mentioned earlier concerning horse and non-horse, and it is the one that prevents the combination of its two sides upon a subject, even if it does not prevent that in a subject. Then opposition was transferred from considering predication upon the subject to considering existence in the subject, so it made the state of things share in a universal or particular, existing in it potentially, like affirmation and negation, whose subjects are the predicates and the subjects alternating and not combining together.
This is by virtue of predication, and there is no predication or position in existence, and some of it is from outside. Among that, there is what shares in a universal, and among it what shares in a particular, and the shared thing is a nature that potentially is both matters, but they do not combine in it but rather alternate upon it. So opposites are said of the matters that were previously mentioned in the sense that they share in a subject that they may exist in it, not that they combine in it. Thus the meaning of this opposition is like a genus for its divisions as species. Then it becomes easy for the author of the Categories to divide in the appropriate manner and say: 'The opposite is either an essence predicated in relation to what is opposite to it; that is the opposition between relatives as passed before, or it is not; that is either that the subject is capable of transitioning from one of the two sides specifically to the other without reversal' — and this is the opposition between privation and possession; for if (with reversal, that is counted in the Categories among contraries as passed). (1) Perihermeneias of Aristotle, p. 118 ff.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of opposition (taqabul) in logic. The author begins by distinguishing opposition that occurs in predication (qawl) and implication (damir), noting that this type does not prevent the combination of opposites in one subject when considered as existing in it rather than being predicated of it. For example, smell and taste are opposites in the sense that smell is not taste, yet they can coexist in the same object (like a fruit that has both smell and taste). The author then moves to other types of opposition: motion/rest, heat/cold, fatherhood/sonship, even/odd. He points out that these are opposites but their forms differ. Horse and non-horse are not contradictories because there is no truth value; they are not relatives nor contraries either. The discussion of even and odd shows that they share a genus (number) but one is the privation of the other. The author then states that darkness is merely absence of light, evil absence of good, rest absence of motion, indicating that these are privations (adam), not positive contraries. However, he notes that many predecessors treat them as contraries due to their definitions. The passage then transitions to a transfer of opposition from predication to existence in a subject, leading to the classification in Aristotle's Categories: opposition between relatives, between privation and possession, and between contraries (with or without reversal). The author ends by referencing Perihermeneias. Throughout, the commentary should emphasize the nuanced distinctions: not all opposition is contradiction; privation is a distinct type where the subject can lack a property it normally possesses; and the classification helps in understanding logical relations in Islamic sciences like fiqh (e.g., purity vs. impurity as privation).

The Transfer of Opposition: From Predication to Existence
Author explains the transfer from predication to existence in opposition classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the distinction between opposition in predication (qawl) and opposition in existence (wujud) and how this affects the classification in Aristotle's Categories.

Core Idea
Opposition considered in existence differs from opposition in predication, leading to the Categories classification.

The passage transitions from discussing opposition in terms of predication (how terms are said of a subject) to opposition in terms of existence (how properties actually exist in a subject). This shift is key to Aristotle's division of opposites into relatives, privation/possession, contraries, and contradictories. Understanding this helps Islamic scholars correctly apply logical categories to theological and legal concepts.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two modes of opposition: predication vs existence
The text states: 'ثم نقل التقابل عن اعتبار الحمل على الموضوع إلى اعتبار الموجود في الموضوع' – opposition was transferred from considering predication to considering existence. Identify examples of each mode.
2
Analyze opposition in predication
In predication, opposites like smell and taste can coexist in a subject (e.g., a sweet-smelling fruit). They are opposed only in meaning, not in existence. The text says: 'لا يمنع اجتماع ما يقع عليه من المتقابلين في موضوع واحد بأن يكونا فيه'.
3
Analyze opposition in existence
In existence, opposites like motion and rest cannot coexist in the same subject at the same time. They alternate. The text says: 'فجعل حال الأشياء تشترك في عام أو خاص تكون موجودة فيه بالقوة كالإيجاب والسلب'.
4
Connect to the Categories classification
The passage concludes that the author of the Categories divides opposites based on existence in a subject: relatives (both exist with mutual relation), privation/possession (one exists, the other is absence in a capable subject), contraries (both exist but alternate), and contradictories (not explicitly listed but implied in alternation of affirmation/negation).

التقابل فيهما في القول والضمير فقط . ومن خواص هذا القسم أنه لا يمنع اجتماع ما يقع عليه من المتقابلين في موضوع واحد بأن يكونا فيه لا بأن يكونا عليه لأن الرائحة مثلا ليست طعمًا؛ وتقابل الطعم من حيث ليس طعما، ويجتمعان في موضوع على سبيل الوجود فيه. فكل ما لم يجتمع على سبيل الوجود فيه فليس يجتمع في موضوع على سبيل القول عليه ولا ينعكس. وأما في الغير كما في الحركة والسكون والحرارة والبرودة والأبوة والبنوة والزوج والفرد مثلا فإنها من المتقابلات أيضا لكن صور هذه الأشياء متخالفة؛ فإن الفرس جوهر دون اللافرس والفرس واللافرس ليس تقابلهما تقابل النقيضين؛ إذ لا صدق هناك ولا كذب ولا تقابل المتضايفين أيضا، ولا تقابل الضدين كذلك لما مر في شأن التضايف والتضاد.
وأما الزوج والفرد فليس لهما موضوع واحد يتعاقبان عليه بل جنس واحد الموضوعين لهما لا يفارقانه. فموضوع الزوجية وهو العدد قد أخذ مع سلب الزوجية التي هي الانقسام بمتساويين؛ ووضع لذلك اسم وجودي هو الفرد. [۱] وعلى هذا في الحركة والسكون والذكورة والأنوثة والنور والظلمة والخير والشر ونحو ذلك فإن الفرد كما يفهم أن الفردية معنى وجودي وليس كذلك فكذلك في جميع هذه الصور: فالظلمة عدم النور لا غير. وكذلك الشر عدم الخير، والسكون عدم الحركة هلم جرا وليس هذا الموضع موضع تحقيقه؛ إذ ذاك في صناعة أخرى. وإنما تعد هذه الأمور من الأضداد في هذا الموضع بناء على التعاريف عند السلف. فإن الجمهور منهم إما أن يعتقدوا أن هذه كلها أمور وجودية أو يعتقدوها أعداما ويعدوها من الأضداد لما أن الضدين عندهم هما اللذان لا يجتمعان في موضوع بل يتعاقبان وجودين كل واحد منهما أو لا وجودين. وأما في المتضايفين فليس بحسب التعاقب فيهما على موضوع أو اشتراكهما في موضوع حتى يكون الموضوع الذي هو علة لأمر ما يلزمه إمكان أن يصير فيه معلولا أو يكون هناك موضوع مشترك وإن كانت العلية والمعلولية من هذا الباب. وبالجملة فجميع الأشياء المتباينة الطبائع تكون متقابلة من حيث إن كل واحد منها ليس هو الآخر. وهذا هو التقابل الذي مر ذكره في الفرس واللافرس، وهو الذي يمنع اجتماع طرفيه على موضوع وإن لم يمنع ذلك في موضوع. ثم نقل التقابل عن اعتبار الحمل على الموضوع إلى اعتبار الموجود في الموضوع فجعل حال الأشياء تشترك في عام أو خاص تكون موجودة فيه بالقوة كالإيجاب والسلب اللذين موضوعهما المحمولات والموضوعات متعاقبة ولا تجتمع معًا.
وهذا بحكم القول، وليس في الوجود حمل ولا وضع وبعضه يكون من خارج. فمن ذلك ما تكون الشركة فيه في عام ومنها ما يكون في خاص ويكون المشترك فيه طبيعة هي بالقوة كلا الأمرين لكن لا يجتمعان فيه بل يتعاقبان عليه. فالمتقابلات مما يقال على الأمور التي سبق ذكرها بمعنى أنها اشتراك في موضوع لها أن يوجد فيه لا أنها تجتمع فيه فيكون معنى هذا التقابل كالجنس الأقسام له كالأنواع: وحينئذ يسهل على صاحب قاطيغورياس أن يقسم على الوجه الذي ينبغي فيقول: (المتقابل إما أن يكون ماهية مقولة بالقياس إلى ما هو مقابل له؛ وذلك هو التقابل بين المتضايفين كما مر من قبل، وإما أن لا يكون؛ وذلك إما أن يكون الموضوع صالحا للانتقال من أحد الطرفين بعينه إلى الآخر من غير انعكاس)، وهذا هو التقابل بين العدم والملكة فإنه إذا كان (مع الانعكاس فذلك يعد في قاطيغورياس من الأضداد كما مر). (۱) باري أرمينياس لأرسطو طاليس، ص۱۱۸ إلخ.
The opposition in them is only in predication and implication. And from the properties of this division is that it does not prevent the combination of what falls under the opposites in one subject inasmuch as they are in it, not inasmuch as they are predicated of it, because the smell, for example, is not taste; and the opposition of taste is inasmuch as it is not taste, and they combine in a subject in the manner of existence in it. So everything that does not combine in the manner of existence in it does not combine in a subject in the manner of predication of it, and the converse does not hold. As for the other, like motion and rest, heat and cold, fatherhood and sonship, even and odd, for example, they are also among the opposites, but the forms of these things are different. For the horse is a substance, unlike non-horse, and the opposition between horse and non-horse is not the opposition of contradictories, since there is no truth and falsehood there, nor is it the opposition of relatives, nor the opposition of contraries, as has passed in the discussion of relation and contrariety.
As for even and odd, they do not have a single subject that they alternate upon, but rather a single genus is the subject for both, not departing from them. So the subject of evenness, which is number, has been taken with the privation of evenness, which is division into two equal parts; and a positive name, 'odd', is assigned to that. [1] Similarly for motion and rest, masculinity and femininity, light and darkness, good and evil, and the like. For the individual, as it is understood that individuality is a positive notion, it is not so; likewise in all these forms: darkness is merely absence of light. Similarly, evil is absence of good, rest is absence of motion, and so on. But this is not the place to verify it, for that belongs to another science. Rather these matters are counted among contraries in this place based on the definitions of the predecessors. For the majority of them either believe that all these are positive matters, or they consider them privations and count them among contraries, because according to them the two contraries are those that do not combine in a subject but alternate, both being existent or non-existent. As for relatives, it is not according to alternation upon a subject or their sharing in a subject, such that the subject that is the cause of something entails the possibility of its becoming an effect in it, or there is a common subject, even if causality and effecthood are from this category. In summary, all things of different natures are opposite inasmuch as each one is not the other. This is the opposition that was mentioned earlier concerning horse and non-horse, and it is the one that prevents the combination of its two sides upon a subject, even if it does not prevent that in a subject. Then opposition was transferred from considering predication upon the subject to considering existence in the subject, so it made the state of things share in a universal or particular, existing in it potentially, like affirmation and negation, whose subjects are the predicates and the subjects alternating and not combining together.
This is by virtue of predication, and there is no predication or position in existence, and some of it is from outside. Among that, there is what shares in a universal, and among it what shares in a particular, and the shared thing is a nature that potentially is both matters, but they do not combine in it but rather alternate upon it. So opposites are said of the matters that were previously mentioned in the sense that they share in a subject that they may exist in it, not that they combine in it. Thus the meaning of this opposition is like a genus for its divisions as species. Then it becomes easy for the author of the Categories to divide in the appropriate manner and say: 'The opposite is either an essence predicated in relation to what is opposite to it; that is the opposition between relatives as passed before, or it is not; that is either that the subject is capable of transitioning from one of the two sides specifically to the other without reversal' — and this is the opposition between privation and possession; for if (with reversal, that is counted in the Categories among contraries as passed). (1) Perihermeneias of Aristotle, p. 118 ff.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of opposition (taqabul) in logic. The author begins by distinguishing opposition that occurs in predication (qawl) and implication (damir), noting that this type does not prevent the combination of opposites in one subject when considered as existing in it rather than being predicated of it. For example, smell and taste are opposites in the sense that smell is not taste, yet they can coexist in the same object (like a fruit that has both smell and taste). The author then moves to other types of opposition: motion/rest, heat/cold, fatherhood/sonship, even/odd. He points out that these are opposites but their forms differ. Horse and non-horse are not contradictories because there is no truth value; they are not relatives nor contraries either. The discussion of even and odd shows that they share a genus (number) but one is the privation of the other. The author then states that darkness is merely absence of light, evil absence of good, rest absence of motion, indicating that these are privations (adam), not positive contraries. However, he notes that many predecessors treat them as contraries due to their definitions. The passage then transitions to a transfer of opposition from predication to existence in a subject, leading to the classification in Aristotle's Categories: opposition between relatives, between privation and possession, and between contraries (with or without reversal). The author ends by referencing Perihermeneias. Throughout, the commentary should emphasize the nuanced distinctions: not all opposition is contradiction; privation is a distinct type where the subject can lack a property it normally possesses; and the classification helps in understanding logical relations in Islamic sciences like fiqh (e.g., purity vs. impurity as privation).

Key Takeaway
Opposition can be considered either in predication (where opposites may coexist) or in existence (where they alternate), and the Categories classification is based on existence.

Classification of Opposition in the Categories
Author explains the concept of opposition and its classification into mutual, privation/possession, and contraries according to the Categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three types of real opposition (mutual, privation/possession, contraries) as defined in the Categories and apply them to examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Opposition is more than logical negation—it includes real relationships in subjects with potential.

In the Categories, Aristotle classified opposition into several types. However, Muslim logicians refined this by distinguishing purely logical opposition (like 'horse and not-horse') from real opposition that occurs in subjects. This passage explains how mutual opposition (taddayuf), privation and possession ('adam wa malaka), and contraries (adhad) are divisions of a single genus, each defined by whether the subject can undergo change with or without reversal. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for precise reasoning in Kalam and Fiqh, where terms like 'sight and blindness' or 'heat and cold' are regularly used.

Guided Path
1
Locate the term being classified
Find the word 'taqabul' (opposition) in the Arabic text. Note that it is treated as a genus (jins) that encompasses three types.
The passage begins by discussing a previous notion of opposition (horse/not-horse) to contrast with real opposition.
2
Identify the criterion for real opposition
Observe that real opposition requires a shared subject in which the opposites can alternate. Logical opposition does not require a shared subject—it is just 'not being the other'.
The text says: 'things with different natures are opposed insofar as each one is not the other'—that is logical opposition.
3
Extract the three types from the classification
The author follows the Categories: (1) mutual: each is defined by relation to the other (e.g., double/half); (2) privation/possession: subject changes without reversal (e.g., sight→blindness); (3) contraries: subject changes with reversal (e.g., cold→heat).
The text uses the condition of 'reversal' (انعكاس) to distinguish contraries from privation/possession.
4
Apply the classification to an Islamic example
Take a pair from Fiqh: 'halal' and 'haram'. They are contraries because a ruling can reverse from halal to haram (e.g., permissibility of a substance before and after prohibition).
This shows how the logical tool is used in Usul al-Fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موضوع أو اشتراكهما في موضوع حتى يكون الموضوع الذي هو علة لأمر ما يلزمه إمكان أن يصير فيه معلولا أو يكون هناك موضوع مشترك وإن كانت العلية والمعلولية من هذا الباب. وبالجملة فجميع الأشياء المتباينة الطبائع تكون متقابلة من حيث إن كل واحد منها ليس هو الآخر. وهذا هو التقابل الذي مرَّ ذكره في الفرس واللا فرس، وهو الذي يمنع اجتماع طرفيه على موضوع وإن لم يمنع ذلك في موضوع. ثم نقل التقابل عن اعتبار الحمل على الموضوع إلى اعتبار الموجود في الموضوع فجعل حال الأشياء تشترك في عام أو خاص تكون موجودة فيه بالقوة كالإيجاب والسلب اللذين موضوعهما المحمولات والموضوعات متعاقبة ولا تجتمع معًا. وهذا بحكم القول، وليس في الوجود حمل ولا وضع وبعضه يكون من خارج. فمن ذلك ما تكون الشركة فيه في عام ومنها ما يكون في خاص ويكون المشترك فيه طبيعة هي بالقوة كلا الأمرين لكن لا يجتمعان فيه بل يتعاقبان عليه. فالمتقابلات مما يقال على الأمور التي سبق ذكرها بمعنى أنها اشتراك في موضوع لها أن يوجد فيه لا أنها تجتمع فيه فيكون معنى هذا التقابل كالجنس الأقسام له كالأنواع: وحينئذ يسهل على صاحب قاطيغورياس أن يقسم على الوجه الذي ينبغي فيقول: «المتقابل إما أن يكون ماهية مقولة بالقياس إلى ما هو مقابل له؛ وذلك هو التقابل بين المتضايفين كما مر من قبل، وإما أن لا يكون؛ وذلك إما أن يكون الموضوع صالحًا للانتقال من أحد الطرفين بعينه إلى الآخر من غير انعكاس»، وهذا هو التقابل بين العدم والملكة فإنه إذا كان (۱) مع الانعكاس فذلك يعد في قاطيغورياس من الأضداد كما مرَّ.
A subject or their sharing in a subject so that the subject which is a cause for something necessitates the possibility that it becomes an effect in it, or there is a shared subject even if causality and effectuality come from this category. In summary, all things with different natures are opposed insofar as each one is not the other. This is the opposition previously mentioned in 'horse and not-horse', which prevents the co-presence of its two extremes in a subject, even if it does not prevent that in a subject. Then opposition was transferred from considering predication to a subject to considering existence in a subject, so it made the state of things share in a common or specific [subject] existing in it potentially, like affirmation and negation whose subjects are predicates and subjects alternating and not coexisting. This is according to statement, and in existence there is no predication nor position, and some of it is from outside. Some of that has sharing in a common [subject] and some has it in a specific [subject], and the shared in it is a nature which is potentially both matters, but they do not coexist in it but alternate upon it. So the opposed things are said of the matters previously mentioned, meaning that they share in a subject that they exist in it, not that they coexist in it. So the meaning of this opposition is like a genus for its divisions as species: Then it becomes easy for the author of the Categories to divide in the appropriate manner and say: 'The opposite is either a quiddity predicated in relation to its opposite, and that is the opposition between mutuals as previously mentioned, or it is not; and if not, either the subject is suitable for transition from one specific extreme to the other without reversal', and that is the opposition between privation and possession, and if it is with reversal, then that is counted in the Categories among the contraries as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies opposition (taqabul). The author begins by explaining that things of different natures are opposed simply by not being each other, illustrated by 'horse and not-horse'. This is an immediate opposition that prevents co-presence in a subject but only in logical terms. Then the discussion shifts to real opposition based on existence in a subject: things share a subject potentially, like affirmation and negation, which alternate and never coexist. This leads to the classification from the Categories: opposition is either mutual (taddayuf) such as father and son, or it is not mutual; if not, it is either privation/possession (al-'adam wa al-malaka) where the subject can change from one to the other without reversal (e.g., sight and blindness), or if there is reversal, it is contraries (adhad) like heat and cold. The author emphasizes that these are divisions of opposition under a common genus.

The Categories classifies opposition into three real types based on the subject's capacity: mutuals, privation/possession, and contraries, distinguished by whether reversal occurs.

Possession, Privation, and the Objection on Heat and Cold
Author details possession and privation, divides real privation, and raises an objection on heat and cold.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define possession and real privation, classify real privation by genus/species/person, and reconstruct the objection that heat and cold are mutual opposites rather than contraries.

Core Idea
Possession is actual capacity; real privation is its absence in a prepared subject; heat and cold challenge the boundary between contrariety and relativity.

This passage from a logic textbook explains two key types of opposition: possession (malaka) and privation ('adam). The author distinguishes possession from mere potentiality and clarifies that real privation is not just absence but absence with the removal of preparedness. He then divides real privation into categories based on whether the absence is relative to the genus, species, or individual. Finally, he raises a critical objection: heat and cold appear to be opposites only in relation to each other, which would make them mutuals (taddayuf) rather than contraries (tadhad). This objection questions the traditional separation of these types of opposition.

Guided Path
1
Define possession (malaka) and distinguish it from mere potentiality
The author says possession is not like 'first potentiality' but rather an actualized capacity that is present whenever the owner wishes. Identify the phrase 'متى شاء صاحبها موجودة' and note that this timeliness distinguishes possession from simple potentiality.
Possession is stable and actual; mere potentiality is not yet actualized.
2
Understand the definition of real privation ('adam haqiqi)
Real privation is the removal of an existential meaning from a matter prepared to receive it, along with the removal of that preparedness. The example of blindness shows it is not mere absence (like a rock lacking sight) but absence from a naturally capable subject.
The phrase 'مع ارتفاع هذا التهيؤ' indicates removal of preparedness.
3
Classify real privation by relation to genus, species, and person
Find the three divisions: (1) relative to genus but not species (e.g., femininity as absence of masculinity in animals), (2) relative to species but not person (e.g., lack of beard in a woman), (3) relative to person, either before time (beardless youth) or at time (alopecia). Note that some privations can cease (alopecia) and some cannot (blindness).
The examples anchor abstract categories in observable cases.
4
Analyze the objection: heat is opposite only relative to cold
The objector says heat alone is not an opposite (tadd) but becomes opposite in relation to cold. Therefore, heat as an opposite is a quiddity predicated in relation to another (مضافة), making it a mutual (mutaddayif). The author presents this as a challenge to the standard classification.
Focus on the logical move: if opposite implies relation, then contrariety and mutuality may be the same.
5
Evaluate the implication: does tadhad collapse into taddayuf?
The passage ends without a resolution. Consider whether the objection forces us to treat all contraries as relatives or whether contrariety can still be distinct. This step requires you to think critically about the categories of opposition.
The text says 'فيكون التضاد والتضايف إما شيء واحد أو التضاد شيء داخل تحت التضايف' indicating two possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المشهور من الملكة ليس مثل الإبصار ولا مثل القوة الأولى التي تُقوي على أن يكون لها بصر بل أن يكون القوة على الإبصار متى شاء صاحبها موجودة والمشهور من العدم هو ارتفاع هذا المعنى عن المادة المتهيئة لقبوله في الوقت الذي من شأنها أن يكون لها مع ارتفاع هذا التهيؤ مثل العمى للبصر مثلا. فإن العمى ليس عدم البصر فقط؛ فإن الجزء الذي لم يفقح هو عادم البصر وليس بأعمى بل العمى عدم البصر في وقت إمكانه وتهيؤ الموضوع له مع ارتفاع التهيؤ ولا يعود البصر البتة فالملكة مما تستحيل إلى العدم بخلاف العدم فإنه لا يستحيل إلى الملكة. وأما العدم في الحقيقي فهو عدم كل معنى وجودي يكون ممكناً للشيء إما بالقياس إلى جنسه أو نوعه أو شخصه قبل الوقت أو في الوقت إما بالقياس إلى الجنس لا إلى النوع فذلك كالأنوثة التي هي عدم الذكورة الممكنة لجنس الحيوان وكالفردية التي هي عدم الزوجية الممكنة لجنس العدد وإما بالقياس إلى النوع لا إلى الشخص فذلك كعدم اللحية للمرأة الممكنة لنوع الإنسان، وإما إلى الشخص؛ فذلك إما قبل الوقت كحال الأمرد فإن عدم اللحية هو عدم قبل الوقت، وإما في الوقت كانتشار الشعر بداء الثعلب؛ إذ عدم في الوقت. والعدم في الوقت قد يزول كهذا، وقد لا يزول كالعمى. وقد مرّ من قبل أن السكون والظلمة والجهل والشر كلها أعدام حقيقية، فهذه هي أقسام التقابل بحسب المشهور والحقيقة. ثم لقائل أن يقول إن الحرارة مثلاً وحدها لا تكون ضدا بل تكون بالقياس إلى البرودة وحينئذ تكون مضافة فإنها وإن لم تكن من حيث هي حرارة من المضاف. فلا تكون أيضًا بمضادة فهي إذن من حيث هي ضد ماهية مقولة بالقياس إلى الغير فتكون مضافة. ثم التضاد والتضايف إما شيء واحد أو التضاد شيء داخل تحت التضايف فلا يكون كالقسم له تحت التقابل. وهنا
Then the commonly known possession is not like sight nor like the first potentiality that strengthens one to have sight, but rather that the power to see whenever its owner wishes is present. And the commonly known privation is the removal of this meaning from the matter prepared to accept it at the time when it is supposed to have it, along with the removal of this preparedness, like blindness for sight for example. Blindness is not merely the absence of sight; the part that has not sprouted is lacking sight but is not blind. Rather, blindness is the absence of sight at the time of its possibility and the subject's preparedness with the removal of preparedness, and sight never returns. So possession is such that it can change into privation, unlike privation which cannot change into possession. As for real privation, it is the absence of any existential meaning that is possible for a thing, either in relation to its genus, species, or person, before the time or at the time. In relation to genus but not species, that is like femininity which is the absence of masculinity possible for the animal genus, and like oddness which is the absence of evenness possible for the number genus. In relation to species but not person, that is like the lack of a beard for a woman possible for the human species. In relation to person, that is either before the time, like the state of the beardless youth, for the lack of a beard is absence before the time, or at the time, like the hair loss due to alopecia, for it is absence at the time. Absence at the time may cease like this, or may not cease like blindness. It has passed before that stillness, darkness, ignorance, and evil are all real privations. These are the divisions of opposition according to common usage and reality. Then someone might say that heat, for example, alone is not an opposite but is opposite relative to cold, and then it becomes mutual. For although it is not, in terms of being heat, from the mutuals, it is also not an opposite. Therefore, in terms of being an opposite, it is a quiddity predicated in relation to another, so it becomes mutual. Then opposition and mutuality either are one thing, or opposition is something subsumed under mutuality, so it is not a division under opposition. And here
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the concepts of possession (malaka) and privation ('adam) as types of opposition. The author distinguishes possession from mere potentiality: possession is an actualized capacity (like sight when one wishes to see), whereas the first potentiality is just a power. Privation is the absence of that capacity in a subject prepared for it, and importantly, it involves the removal of preparedness itself—for example, blindness is not just lack of sight in an eye that never had sight (like a stone), but in an eye that was supposed to see. Possession can change into privation (e.g., blindness), but privation cannot revert to possession. Then real privation is further divided: relative to genus (e.g., femininity is privation of masculinity in animals), to species (e.g., lack of beard in women), and to person, either before time (youth without beard) or at time (alopecia). The author notes that rest, darkness, ignorance, and evil are real privations. Finally, an objection is raised: heat and cold seem to be mutual opposites (taddayuf) because heat is defined relative to cold. The objection suggests that opposition (tadhad) might be subsumed under mutuality, challenging the classification. The text ends mid-argument.

عدم حقيقي

Key Takeaway
The passage clarifies the definitions of possession and real privation, while the objection on heat and cold raises a fundamental question about whether contrariety is a distinct type of opposition or reducible to relativity.

Possession and Privation as Opposites
Author defines possession and privation with blindness example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between possession (malaka) and privation ('adam) and identify the conditions that make a privation true privation rather than mere absence.

Core Idea
Privation is not just absence; it requires a subject with potential at a specific time.

This lesson clarifies the logical opposition between possession and privation. The author defines possession as an active power present at will, and privation as the removal of that power from a subject naturally disposed to have it, at the time it should have it, with the disposition permanently removed. The classic example is blindness vs. mere lack of sight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definienda
Find the two terms being defined: malaka (possessions) and 'adam (privation). The author begins by clarifying what possession is not, then gives a positive definition.
Possession is distinguished from a mere capacity or a first potentiality.
2
Extract the genus and differentia for possession
For malaka: the genus is a power or state (qūwa), and the differentia is that it is present whenever its possessor wills (mata shā'a ṣāḥibuhā mawjūda).
This definition emphasizes active readiness.
3
Extract the genus and differentia for privation
For 'adam: the genus is absence (irtifā' or 'adam), and the differentia is that it is the removal of a meaning from matter disposed to receive it at the proper time, with the disposition itself removed.
The conditions of potentiality, time, and disposition removal are critical.
4
Apply the definitions to the blindness example
Blindness is not mere absence of sight (like in a stone or a closed eye). It is the absence of sight in a subject that had the potential to see, at the time when sight should be present, and with the disposition permanently removed.
This distinguishes true privation from simple negation.
5
Verify the asymmetry between possession and privation
The passage states that possession can change into privation (e.g., sight lost), but privation does not revert to possession (blindness cannot be cured). This is a key logical property.
This asymmetry is part of the definition of this type of opposition.

Source Text

Then the well-known [meaning] of possession is not like sight nor like the first power that strengthens [one] to have sight, but rather that the power to see whenever its possessor wills is present. And the well-known [meaning] of privation is the removal of this meaning from matter disposed to receive it at a time when it is supposed to have it, along with the removal of this disposition, like blindness for sight for example. For blindness is not merely the absence of sight; indeed the part that has not opened is lacking sight and is not blind, rather blindness is the absence of sight at the time of its possibility and the disposition of the subject for it along with the removal of the disposition, and sight never returns. So possession is something that can change into privation, unlike privation for it does not change into possession. As for absolute privation, it is the absence of every existential meaning possible for a thing, either relative to its genus or its species or its individual, before the time or at the time. [Examples] relative to genus not to species: like femininity which is absence of masculinity possible for the genus of animal, and like oddness which is absence of evenness possible for the genus of number. Relative to species not to individual: like absence of beard for a woman possible for the species of man. Relative to individual: either before time like the state of a beardless youth, for absence of beard is absence before time; or at the time like the spreading of hair due to alopecia, for it is absence at the time. And absence at the time may cease like this, or may not cease like blindness. It has previously passed that stillness, darkness, ignorance, and evil are all real privations. These are the divisions of opposition according to the well-known and the real.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage defines two types of opposition: possession (malaka) and privation ('adam). It opens by clarifying that possession is not like sight itself or a mere capacity, but an active power present at will, like the ability to see when one chooses. Privation is then defined not as mere absence, but as the removal of a quality from a subject naturally disposed to have it, at the time it should have it, with the disposition itself gone. Blindness is the example: it is not just lack of sight (as in a stone or a non-developed eye), but loss of sight in a subject that had the potential, with the disposition permanently removed. The passage then explains that possession can change into privation (like losing sight), but privation cannot revert to possession (blindness cannot be cured). Next, 'absolute privation' is introduced: the absence of any possible existential attribute relative to genus, species, or individual, either before or at the proper time. Examples include: femininity relative to the genus animal (absence of masculinity), oddness relative to number (absence of evenness), lack of beard in a woman relative to the species human (she is human but lacks a beard by nature), and lack of beard in a youth before puberty (before the time). The passage ends by noting that stillness, darkness, ignorance, and evil are all real privations, summarising the types of opposition according to common usage and reality.

Key Takeaway
True privation (al-'adam) is not mere absence; it is the removal of a quality from a subject that had the natural potential for it, at the specific time it should be present, with the disposition permanently lost.

Absolute Privation and Its Subdivisions
Author defines absolute privation with subdivisions and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any instance of absolute privation according to genus, species, individual, and time using the examples given in the passage.

Core Idea
Absolute privation is not just absence; it is context-dependent on the level of genus, species, or individual and whether it is before or at the proper time.

After defining basic privation, the author introduces 'absolute privation' (al-'adam al-haqiqi) as the absence of any possible existential attribute relative to the genus, species, or individual of a thing, either before or at the time it should be present. This lesson breaks down the classification with examples like femininity, oddness, and lack of beard, and concludes that stillness, darkness, ignorance, and evil are all real privations.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three levels of privation
Privation can be relative to the genus, species, or individual. Relative to genus means the attribute is possible for the wider category but absent in a particular instance (e.g., femininity relative to animal genus). Relative to species means the attribute is possible for the species but absent in an individual of that species (e.g., beard in a woman). Relative to individual considers the specific entity.
The passage also distinguishes time: before the time (like a youth's beard) and at the time (like hair loss).
2
Analyze the example: femininity and oddness
Femininity is given as the absence of masculinity possible for the genus animal. Oddness is the absence of evenness possible for the genus number. These are privations relative to genus, not species or individual.
The attribute 'masculinity' is possible for animals, so its absence in a female is a privation at the genus level.
3
Analyze the example: absence of beard in a woman
A woman lacks a beard that is possible for the species human (since men have beards). This is privation relative to species, not individual.
The passage says 'possible for the species of man' meaning the species human.
4
Analyze the examples relative to individual: before and at the time
A beardless youth lacks a beard before the time it would naturally appear (before puberty). Alopecia (hair loss) is absence at the time it should be present. Some absences at the time may cease (alopecia can be treated), while others do not (blindness).
Time is a key differentiator for individual privation.
5
Identify examples of real privations
The passage lists stillness, darkness, ignorance, and evil as real privations (absolute privations). This connects to broader philosophical discussions.
These are privations of motion, light, knowledge, and good respectively.

How the Author Refutes the Equation of Contrariety and Correlativity
Author refutes objection equating contrariety and correlativity, clarifying their distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objector's argument and the author's refutation distinguishing contrariety from correlativity.

Core Idea
Contrariety is not correlativity: the relation between contraries is accidental, not essential.

A common confusion in logic is thinking that because contraries like heat and cold are considered in relation to each other, they are essentially relatives. The author engages dialectically with an objector who pushes this point, and then refutes it with a precise analysis of how the subjects of predication differ. Follow the steps below to see how the objection is met.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
Read the first sentence of the Arabic: the objector says heat alone is not a contrary, but becomes so relative to cold, and thus becomes a relative. His conclusion is that contrariety and correlativity are either the same or contrariety is under correlativity.
The objector is attacking the standard division of taqabul (opposition) into four species.
2
Locate the author's response starting 'lakin naqulu'
The author says: heat is considered together with cold; from the perspective of being heat, it is a contrary to cold. Then it is taken as a contrary again and becomes a relative. This shows that the meaning of 'contrary' applies to both, but the meaning of 'relative' does not apply to both because neither is a quiddity said essentially with reference to the other.
The author distinguishes the two considerations: contrariety is about the quiddity itself, while relativity is an accidental relation that arises from the consideration of opposition.
3
Understand the subject distinction
The author explains that the subject of contrariety is different from the subject of relativity. The subject of contrariety when taken as contrary becomes the cause of relativity, but not vice versa. Therefore contrariety is not correlativity.
This is a key logical move: the same thing can be subject to different predicates under different aspects (bi-‘tibar).
4
Address the second doubt about opposition
The objector also argued that opposition itself is relative. The author responds: opposites are accidentally relative, not essentially. Possession and privation are also not relatives. Every correlative is an opposite, but not every opposite is a correlative. So correlativity is not more general than opposition.
This clarifies that tadayuf is a species under taqabul, not the other way around.
