Distinguishing True Contraries from Privation and Correlatives
Author defines true contraries and distinguishes them from privation/possession and correlatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify true contraries, privation/possession pairs, and correlatives in Islamic logical texts, and distinguish them based on substrate and mutual implication.

Core Idea
Contraries share a substrate but do not entail each other; correlatives entail each other; privation is absence in a receptive subject.

In Islamic logic, not all opposites are alike. The author of this passage clarifies the specific conditions for true contrariety, distinguishing it from privation/possession and correlative opposition. Understanding these distinctions is essential for precise reasoning about categories like black/white, justice/injustice, and health/sickness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of opposition being discussed
The passage defines true contraries (تضاد) and contrasts them with privation/possession (عدم وملكة) and correlatives (متضايفان). Determine which type is the main focus and how the others serve as counterexamples.
The author begins by noting that the division of substrate types applies only to contrariety, not to privation/possession.
2
Extract the defining conditions of true contraries
True contraries must: (1) share a single substrate, (2) not coexist but alternate, (3) have extreme difference between them. Example: whiteness and blackness share the natural body as substrate.
Lukewarm (فاتر) is not a true contrary of hot because it is intermediate, not extreme.
3
Distinguish privation/possession from contrariety
Privation is the absence of a quality in a subject naturally capable of having it, regardless of time or return. Example: blindness is privation of sight in a seeing creature. Unlike contraries, privation/possession does not require a single substrate or mutual exclusion in the same way.
The author stresses that privation does not involve extreme difference between two positive qualities.
4
Distinguish correlatives from contraries
Correlatives (like father and son) necessarily imply each other's existence. If one exists, the other must exist. This is not true for contraries: health implies the possibility of sickness, not its actuality.
The author gives the example: if all people were healthy, it would not necessitate sickness, but a father necessarily implies a son.
5
Analyze the substrate (محل) relationships
The passage shows various substrate possibilities for contraries: common genus (body for black/white), specific nature (human soul for justice/injustice), genus (number for even/odd), different genera (chastity vs debauchery), or the terms themselves as genera (good/evil). This illustrates that contraries can have different levels of substrate.
Understanding the substrate helps determine whether a pair qualifies as true contraries.

And if we say Zayd is healthy, it is impossible that he be sick. This is contrary to what is the case with correlatives, for one of them absolutely necessitates the existence of the other, and it is not impossible that what is a father is also a son. Then the substrate in correlatives is one. So perhaps that one meaning is more general than a species, like blackness and whiteness, for their subject is the natural body. And perhaps it is of one species, like injustice and justice, for their subject is not every soul but the human soul. And perhaps the subject for two contraries is a genus, like number for evenness and oddness for example. And perhaps they are in one genus, like blackness and whiteness in color. And perhaps they are in two different genera, like chastity and debauchery, one of which is from the genus of virtue and the other from the genus of vice. Nevertheless, we should give a true indication and say: True contraries are the things that share one subject, like blackness and whiteness, not like rest and movement; and the two opposite things from them do not coexist but alternate, and between them is extreme difference, not like lukewarm and hot. As for privation and possession, the true [sense] of privation is that a thing is non-existent in a subject that is by nature receptive to its existence, considered insofar as it is so, whether the non-existent is what you call possession or something else, whether it returns or does not return, and whether [the privation] is before the time or after. And from it is what is more general than that, which is the non-existence of a thing from what is in its nature to accompany it, whether that nature is individual, specific, or generic, as has passed before.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes types of opposition, focusing on contrariety (tadd) and contrasting it with privation/possession ('adam wa malaka) and correlatives (mutadha'ifin). The author begins by noting that in true contrariety, the two extremes are not like the state of lukewarm (which is between hot and cold), while in other oppositions like courage (which has only one opposite, cowardice), the structure differs. He then states that the division (of substrate being one or multiple) applies to contrariety but not to privation/possession. He recalls a previous point: contrary terms do not imply each other's existence universally (e.g., if everyone were healthy, it doesn't necessitate illness), unlike correlatives (e.g., father implies son). The author then examines the substrate (mahall) for correlatives and contraries, showing various cases: sometimes the substrate is a common genus (like natural body for black/white), sometimes specific (human soul for justice/injustice), sometimes a genus (number for even/odd), sometimes different genera (virtue vs vice), and sometimes the terms themselves are genera (good/evil). He clarifies that good and evil are not essential but accidental to dispositions, and they belong to the category of quality. Finally, he defines true contraries as sharing a single subject and mutually exclusive, and contrasts this with privation/possession, where privation is the absence of a quality in a subject naturally capable of having it, without temporal restrictions.

Key Terms
تضاد (tadd): Opposition of two things that share the same substrate, cannot coexist, and have extreme difference between them. [Note: Standard Aristotelian term; stable across the tradition.]
عدم وملكة ('adam wa malaka): Opposition where one term is the absence of a quality that a subject is naturally capable of having, without temporal conditions. [Note: Classical meaning; consistent in logic texts.]
متضايفان (mutadha'ifan): Correlatives: two things whose existence necessarily imply each other, e.g., father and son. [Note: Stable term for relational opposites.]
محل (mahall): The underlying subject or substrate in which opposing attributes exist. [Note: General term for subject of inherence; no shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
True contraries share a single substrate, cannot coexist, and have extreme difference; they are distinct from privation/possession (absence in a receptive subject) and correlatives (mutual implication).

The Five Kinds of Priority and Posteriority
Author enumerates and explains the five types of temporal and logical priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the five types of priority (temporal, natural, honor, absolute rank, relative rank) and identify which type applies to a given Islamic example.

Core Idea
Priority is not just time; nature, honor, and rank also define what comes first.

This passage from the logic text explains that priority and posteriority are not limited to time. Following Aristotle's Categories, the author lists five types: temporal, natural, honorary, and two kinds of rank. Understanding these distinctions helps clarify relations in Islamic sciences, such as the priority of the Prophet over companions or the priority of genus over species.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the context
The author places this discussion after opposites, noting that priority/posteriority shares similarities with opposition. He restricts to well-known types, following Aristotle's 'First Teaching'.
This signals that full investigation is beyond elementary study.
2
List the five types
Identify each division: (1) temporal, (2) natural, (3) honorary, (4) absolute rank, (5) relative rank. The text numbers them clearly.
Temporal priority is straightforward; the others need closer attention.
3
Define each type's distinctive feature
For each type, extract how it differs: temporal (age), natural (non-reciprocal dependence, e.g., one prior to two), honor (dignity, e.g., Prophet over Companions), absolute rank (reference point like highest genus), relative rank (depends on direction, e.g., genus prior if top-down, species if bottom-up).
Natural priority involves ontological dependence, not temporal sequence.
4
Analyze the reversibility of rank priority
The author emphasizes that priority in rank is not intrinsic but depends on the direction of relation. Understand that absolute rank has a fixed reference, while relative rank can flip.
This is key: rank priority is relative to the perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في المتقدم والمتأخر] وأما المتقدم والمتأخر فالكلام فيهما يذكر عقيب الكلام في المتقابلات لما أن الحال فيه نحو الحال في المتقابلات أعني أن نعرفه حق الاستقصاء فيه ليس مداخل التعليم فيجب أن نقتصر على إيراد ما هو المشهور منه كما في التعليم الأول؛ وذلك على خمسة أقسام : [1] الأول هو التقدم بالزمان فإن الأكبر سناً أقدم من الذي هو أصغر سنًا منه، [ب] الثاني هو التقدم بالطبع وهو الذي لا يرفع بالتكافؤ في اللزوم كما في الواحد عند الاثنين مثلا، [ت] الثالث التقدم بالشرف كتقدم النبي على الصحابة ونحو ذلك، [ث] الرابع هو الذي يكون في المرتبة إما على الإطلاق وهو الذي تنسب إليه الأشياء فيكون بعضها أقرب منه بالنسبة إلى البعض مثل الجسم الأعلى في حكم الجنسية والنوع السافل في حكم النوعية . وأما بعد الإطلاق مثل الجنس فإنه متقدم على الحيوان إن اعتبرت الإبتداء من الجنس الأعلى، وإنه على العكس إن اعتبرت من النوع السافل. ثم المتقدم بالمرتبة ليس يجب له بذاته أن يكون متقدما بل بحسب اعتبار النسبة؛ ولذلك قد ينقلب الأقدم. وكما أن المرتبة هو
Section: On the Prior and Posterior. As for the prior and posterior, the discussion about them is mentioned after the discussion of opposites because the situation regarding them is similar to the situation regarding opposites—I mean that to know them with thorough investigation is not an entry point to instruction, so we must restrict ourselves to presenting what is well-known about them, as in the First Teaching (Aristotle's Categories). They are of five divisions: [1] The first is priority in time; for the older in age is prior to one who is younger in age. [2] The second is priority in nature; that which is not removed by equivalence in implication, like the one in relation to the two, for example. [3] The third is priority in honor, like the priority of the Prophet over the Companions, and similar. [4] The fourth is that which is in rank, either absolutely—that to which things are related, so some are nearer to it relative to others, like the highest genus in the case of genus and the lowest species in the case of species. And as for relative rank, like the genus, it is prior to 'animal' if you consider starting from the highest genus, and the opposite if you consider starting from the lowest species. Then the prior in rank is not necessarily prior in itself but according to consideration of relation; therefore the prior may be reversed. And just as rank...
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a classification of different types of priority and posteriority (mutaqaddim wa muta'akhkhir). The author notes that this topic follows the discussion of opposites because of their similarity. He says that a full investigation is beyond the scope of this introductory work, so he presents only what is well-known, following Aristotle's Categories (al-Ta'lim al-Awwal). He then enumerates five types. First: temporal priority, e.g., an older person is prior to a younger one. Second: natural or essential priority, which is not reversed by reciprocal implication; for example, the number one is prior to two because two cannot exist without one, but the implication is not equal. Third: priority in honor or dignity, such as the Prophet's priority over the Companions. Fourth: priority in rank or order, which can be absolute (e.g., the highest genus as the starting point of categorization) or relative (e.g., genus is prior to species when considering from top-down, but species is prior when considering from bottom-up). The author emphasizes that priority in rank is not intrinsic but depends on the direction of relation, so it can be reversed. The passage ends abruptly with 'وكما أن المرتبة هو' which likely continues on the next page.

المتقدم والمتأخر
التقدم بالطبع
التقدم بالشرف
التقدم بالمرتبة

The Five Types of Priority and an Objection Regarding Temporal Parts
Author explains five types of priority and responds to an objection about temporal precedence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five types of priority (al-taqaddum) and evaluate the objection that parts of time require a sixth type.

Core Idea
Priority is not only temporal; it has causal, natural, honorary, and rank-based forms.

In this passage, the author enumerates the well-known types of priority and responds to a critic who claims the division is incomplete. Understanding these types is crucial for analyzing relationships in kalam and usul al-fiqh, where causality and priority are central. We will walk through the classification and the dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five types of priority
From the passage, list the five types mentioned: priority by cause (bi'l-illah), by nature (bi't-tab'), by honor (bi'sh-sharaf), by rank (bi'l-martaba), by time (bi'z-zaman). Note that the author also mentions place but the objector focuses on five.
The types are often used to disambiguate discussions in kalam about the priority of the Creator over creation.
2
Understand the objector's challenge
The objector argues that parts of time (azman) have a priority that does not fit any of these five: it is not by cause (cause and effect are simultaneous), not by nature (prior by nature coexists with posterior like one and two, but time parts do not coexist), not by honor, rank, time, or place.
The objector's claim is that a sixth type of priority is needed for temporal parts.
3
Examine the author's response
The author concedes that this priority is not by rank, but claims it does not undermine the presented division, as he only aimed to present the well-known types.
The author does not deny the existence of a sixth type; he simply limits his discussion.
4
Apply the distinction to an Islamic example
Consider the priority of the Preserved Tablet over created events: is this by cause, nature, or time? Reflect on how each type would apply.
This exercise helps solidify the conceptual distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالطبع على المتقدم بالعلية والذات وهذا هو التقدم مثل وجود الإنسان في نفسه. وقول القائل إنه موجود فإنه كلما كان القول بأنه موجود صادقًا فهو موجود. وكلما كان موجودًا فالقول بذلك صادق لكن الناس لا يتحاشون عن أن يقولوا إنه كان أولاً موجوداً ثم كان القول بوجوده صادقًا. وكذلك في مثل حركة اليد وغير ذلك من التقدم بالعلية فإن العلة وإن كانت من حيث هي ذات ومعلولها ذات لا يتقدم ولا يتأخر ولا يكونان معا، وكانت من حيث هي علة لزمها الإضافة والآخر معلول لزمها الإضافة لا يتقدم أيضًا ولا يتأخر بل هما معا فإنه من حيث إن وجوده ليس عن الآخر، ووجود الآخر عنه هو متقدم بالنسبة إلى حال الوجود فيكون له النسبة إلى الوجود من غير توسط وجود الغير، والآخر لا نسبة له إلى الوجود ولا توسط وجود الغير. ثم لقائل أن يقول القسمة لا تكون منحصرة في هذه الخمسة فإن أجزاء الزمان بعضها متقدم على البعض وذلك التقدم ليس بالعلية؛ إذ العلة مع المعلول، وأجزاء الزمان لا يمكن أن يكون كذلك. وليس أيضًا بالطبع لما أن المتقدم بالطبع لا بد وأن يكون موجودًا مع المتأخر نحو الواحد مع الاثنين. ومثل هذه المعية لا يمكن في أجزاء الزمان، وليس أيضًا بالشرف فإن المتقدم بالشرف قد يقارن المتأخر فيه، وليس أيضًا بالمرتبة فإنه لا مرتبة للبعض من أجزاء الزمان على البعض، وليس أيضًا بالزمان وإلا لكان الزمان زمانا ولا بالمكان كذلك؛ وهذا ظاهر. ولما لم يكن من هذه الأنواع كان نوعا غير هذه الأنواع لا محالة. لكنا نقول إنه إذا لم يكن بالمرتبة كان كما ذكر تم لكنه لا يكون قادحًا فيما ذكرناه وهو أن نقتصر على إيراد ما هو المشهور منه؛ ولما عرفت أقسام المتقدم فاعتبر بنفسك أقسام المتأخر وغيره.
By nature, it is prior by cause and essence, and this is the priority such as the existence of man in himself. And the statement of the speaker that he exists: whenever the statement that he exists is true, he exists. And whenever he exists, the statement about that is true, but people do not refrain from saying that he first existed, then the statement about his existence was true. Similarly, in the case of the movement of the hand and other instances of priority by cause, for the cause, although in terms of being an essence and its effect an essence, neither is prior nor posterior nor are they simultaneous, but in terms of being a cause, relation is necessary for it, and the other is an effect, relation is necessary for it, nor is it prior or posterior, rather they are simultaneous. For in terms of its existence not being from the other, and the other's existence from it, it is prior with respect to the state of existence, so it has the relation to existence without the mediation of the existence of the other, while the other has no relation to existence except through the mediation of the existence of the other. Then an objector may say: The division is not confined to these five, for parts of time are prior to each other, and that priority is not by cause, since the cause is simultaneous with the effect, and parts of time cannot be like that. Nor is it by nature, for the prior by nature must exist with the posterior, like the one with the two. And such simultaneity is not possible in parts of time. Nor is it by honor, for the prior by honor may be accompanied by the posterior in it. Nor is it by rank, for there is no rank of some parts of time over others. Nor is it by time, otherwise time would be time, nor by place, and that is obvious. Since it is not of these types, it must be a type other than these types. But we say: If it is not by rank, it is as mentioned, but it does not undermine what we mentioned, namely that we confine ourselves to presenting what is well-known from it. And since you have known the divisions of the prior, consider for yourself the divisions of the posterior and other than it.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage defines the types of priority (al-taqaddum) and defends the classification against an objection. The author begins by explaining priority by cause and essence, using the example of existence: a man's existence is prior to the statement about his existence, because the statement depends on the existence. Similarly, the movement of the hand is prior in the sense of causality. The cause and effect, considered as essences, are simultaneous, but in terms of causality, the cause is prior because its existence does not depend on the effect, whereas the effect depends on the cause. The objector then argues that the division into five types (by cause, nature, honor, rank, time, place) is incomplete because parts of time (e.g., moments) have a priority that is not by cause (since cause and effect are simultaneous), nor by nature (since prior by nature must coexist with posterior, like one and two, which time parts do not), nor by honor, rank, time, or place. Therefore, it must be a sixth type. The author responds that even if this type of priority is not by rank, it does not invalidate the division, because the author only intended to present the well-known types. The passage then invites the reader to deduce the divisions of the posterior and simultaneous from this.

Key Terms
المتقدم
التقدم بالعلية
التقدم بالطبع

Checkpoint

B) Because time parts have priority by rank.

D) Because the author forgot to include a type for time.

Key Takeaway
Priority has multiple senses (causal, natural, honorary, rank-based, temporal), and the division into five types is conventional, not exhaustive.

The Five Types of Simultaneity (al-Ma'iyya)
Author defines simultaneity in time and nature, and distinguishes its subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the five types of simultaneity (al-ma'iyya) and identify which type applies to a given pair of things, using examples from Islamic logic and kalam.

Core Idea
Simultaneity is not just time: things can be together in nature, rank, honor, or causality.

In this passage, the author unpacks the concept of simultaneity (ma'iyya) beyond mere temporal co-occurrence. You will learn how different kinds of 'togetherness' are distinguished in the classical logical tradition, especially in the context of definitions and causal relations. This will sharpen your ability to analyze relationships between concepts and entities in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core definition of simultaneity
Simultaneity is defined as the absence of priority and posteriority between two things. If neither is before or after the other, they are called 'ma'an' (simultaneous). Identify this core idea in the Arabic: 'لم يتقدم أحدهما على الآخر ولا يتأخر'.
This definition applies to all types.
2
List the five types mentioned
The passage explicitly mentions: (1) in time, (2) in nature, (3) in rank, (4) in honor, and (5) in causality. Note that causality is marked as problematic and referred to natural science.
The author also alludes to simultaneity in honor as derived from rank.
3
Distinguish 'in nature' from 'in rank'
Species under a single genus are simultaneous in nature (no natural priority) and in rank (equal distance from the genus). Relatives (e.g., father and son) are simultaneous in nature but have different ranks. See how the text uses 'الأنواع تحت جنس واحد' as an example.
This distinction is crucial for understanding definitions in hadd.
4
Recognize the problematic case: causality
The author calls simultaneity in causality 'problematic' (مشكل) and says it relates to motion, linking it to physics. This shows the limits of logical analysis and the need to defer to other sciences.
You may need to recall basics of natural philosophy.
5
Apply the typology to a concrete example
Consider two species under the same genus, e.g., 'quadruped' and 'biped' under the genus 'animal'. They are simultaneous in nature (neither is prior to the other in essence) and in rank (equally distant from the genus). Are they also simultaneous in honor? Possibly, but that depends on value judgments.
This exercise trains you to use the typology in real logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه لم يتقدم أحدهما على الآخر ولا يتأخر فهما بالضرورة فيقال معا في الزمان ومعا في الطبع : فهما إما أن يكونا مثلا وبين تكافؤ الوجود كما في الأمور الإضافية، وإما أن يكونا منافيين كما في الأنواع تحت جنس واحد ليس لا بهما معا في الطبع فقط بل في المرتبة كما مرَّ . فالمعية في الأنواع معية بإزاء التقدم والتأخر . وأما معيتها في المرتبة فلأنها متساوية في القرب والبعد من المبدأ الذي هو الجنس؛ ذلك أن يعلم من هذا حال معا في الشرف. وأما معا في العلية فذلك مشكل. والكلام فيه في الحركة كما هو حقه يعرف من العلم الطبيعي؛ وإنها أي الحركة ومقولة أن ينفعل سواء.
For neither of them is prior to the other nor posterior, so they are necessarily said to be simultaneous in time and simultaneous in nature: they are either similar and have equivalence of existence, as in relative things, or they are opposite, as in species under a single genus, which are simultaneous not only in nature but also in rank, as has passed. So simultaneity in species is simultaneity in place of priority and posteriority. As for their simultaneity in rank, it is because they are equal in nearness and distance from the principle which is the genus. From this, the state of simultaneity in honor is known. As for simultaneity in causality, that is problematic. The discussion of it in motion, as is its due, is known from natural science; and that motion and the category of being affected are the same.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage clarifies the concept of simultaneity (al-ma'iyya) as the absence of priority and posteriority. The author distinguishes between simultaneity in time and simultaneity in nature. Two things are simultaneous in time if they exist at the same moment. Simultaneity in nature means that neither is naturally prior, like two correlatives (e.g., father and son) or like species under the same genus (e.g., quadruped and biped). Species under a genus are also simultaneous in rank because they are equally distant from the genus. The author notes that simultaneity in honor is derived from this, while simultaneity in causality is problematic and refers to motion, which is studied in natural science. The passage ends by linking motion to the category of being affected.

Key Terms
معا
المعية
الأنواع تحت جنس واحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because they exist at the same time.
B) Because they are equal in nearness and distance from the genus.
C) Because one is prior to the other by nature.
D) Because they are opposite in definition.

Key Takeaway
Simultaneity is a multi-faceted concept in Islamic logic, encompassing temporal, natural, rank-based, honorary, and causal dimensions, each with specific applications in definition and analysis.

Classifying Motion: Categories and Subtypes
Author enumerates types of motion by category and adds motion in position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each type of motion (substance, quality, quantity, place, position) and their subtypes as classified by the author.

Core Idea
Motion includes substantial change, quality change, quantitative change, spatial movement, and even rotational position.

The author presents a systematic classification of motion (al-harakah) based on the Aristotelian categories. He not only lists the main types but also adds a personal insight: motion in position, which is often overlooked. This classification is foundational for later discussions in Kalam and natural philosophy, where the nature of change is central to arguments about the temporality of the world.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five main categories of motion
Read the passage and list the five types: in substance, quality, quantity, place, and position.
The author introduces motion in position as his own addition ('indī).
2
Distinguish between motion in substance and quality
Motion in substance (jawhar) is exemplified by the sperm becoming a human (substantial change). Motion in quality (kayf) is alteration (istihālah), like becoming black or hot.
These are changes in the essence and attributes, respectively.
3
Break down quantitative motion into increase and decrease
Increase includes growth (numuww) by addition and rarefaction (takhalukhul) by expansion. Decrease includes withering (dhubūl) by rarefaction of parts and condensation (takāthuf) without rarefaction.
Note the difference: growth adds substance, rarefaction expands without adding.
4
Explain motion in place and motion in position
Motion in place is translation (naqlah). Motion in position (al-ḥarakah fī al-waḍʿ) is rotation around one's center, which does not involve leaving the place.
The author emphasizes that positional motion is not spatial displacement.
5
Note the methodological remark
The author states that terms like prior, posterior, opposite, and motion are used in teaching categories, but other logical notions (like homonymy, predication) should be studied first.
This shows the author's systematic approach to teaching logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من الحركة ما يكون في الجوهر مثل كون النطفة إنسانًا، ومنها ما يكون في الكيف وهو الاستحالة مثل التسود والتسخن، ومنها ما يكون في الكم وإنه إما إلى الزيادة، وذلك إن كان بالزائد فهو النمو، وإن كان بانبساط الجوهر فهو التخلخل . وإما إلى النقصان، وذلك إن كان بتخلخل الأجزاء فهو الذبول، وإن كان لا بالتخلل فهو التكاثف ومنها ما يكون في المكان فهو النقلة . وعندي أن الحركة نوع آخر وهو الحركة في الوضع كحركة الجسم على مركز نفسه مستديرا؛ فإن هذه الحركة لا تكون مكانية. وربما تكون لا في مكان، وإن كانت في مكان لم يفارق المتمكن مكانه بالكلية بل يكون مستبدلا للوضع دون الأين. وبالجملة فالمتقدم والمتأخر والمقابل والمع والحركة كلها من الألفاظ المستعملة في تعليم المقولات. وأما النظر في حال الاتفاق والتواطؤ وما على موضوع وما في موضوع وغير ذلك فكان محتاجًا تقديمه على المقولات؛ إذ لا بد من استعمالها في تعليم المقولات؛ فاعتبر بما عرفت فإن في هذا القدر كفاية في هذا الباب، والله أعلم بالصواب وإليه المرجع والمآب.
Then of motion, some is in substance, such as the sperm becoming a human; some is in quality, which is alteration, such as becoming black or hot; some is in quantity, and it is either to increase, and that if it is by addition then it is growth, and if it is by expansion of the substance then it is rarefaction; or to decrease, and that if it is by rarefaction of parts then it is withering, and if it is not by rarefaction then it is condensation; and some is in place, which is translation. And in my view, there is another type of motion, namely motion in position, such as the motion of a body around its own center in a circle; for this motion is not spatial. It may be not in place, and if it is in place, the thing in place does not leave its place entirely but rather changes its position without changing its where. In summary, the prior, posterior, opposite, simultaneous, and motion are all terms used in teaching the categories. As for examining the situation of agreement, equivocation, what is on a subject, what is in a subject, and so on, that needed to be presented before the categories, for they must be used in teaching the categories. So consider what you have known, for in this amount is sufficient in this chapter, and Allah knows best what is correct, and to Him is the return and the final abode.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of motion (al-harakah) according to the Aristotelian categories. The author lists motion in substance (e.g., generation), quality (alteration), quantity (increase: growth and rarefaction; decrease: withering and condensation), and place (translation). Then the author adds a personal view: there is another type, motion in position (al-harakah fi al-wad'), exemplified by rotation around an axis, which is not spatial translocation. Finally, the author concludes that these terms (priority, posteriority, opposition, simultaneity, motion) are used in teaching the categories, but other topics like homonymy, synonymy, and predication need to be studied first. This serves as a methodological note.

الحركة في الوضع

Key Takeaway
Motion is categorized into five types: in substance, quality, quantity, place, and position; the author emphasizes that positional motion is distinct from spatial translation and recommends studying basic logical concepts before the categories.

Two Types of Togetherness: Species and Rank
Distinguishes two types of togetherness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between togetherness in species and togetherness in rank based on their relation to genus.

Core Idea
Togetherness in species is relative; togetherness in rank is absolute.

In this passage, the author examines what it means for two things to be 'together' in a categorical sense. He identifies two types: togetherness in species, which depends on priority and posteriority, and togetherness in rank, which is based on equal distance from a common genus. Understanding this distinction is crucial for classifying relations within categories, a tool used in Islamic kalam and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two kinds of togetherness
Read the first sentences: two things can be together in nature and time either as equivalent in existence (like correlatives) or as opposite species under one genus. The latter includes two subtypes: togetherness in species and togetherness in rank.
2
Extract the criterion for togetherness in species
The author states that togetherness in species is relative to priority and posteriority. That is, species are ordered in a sequence relative to a genus.
For example, 'primary' and 'secondary' substances.
3
Extract the criterion for togetherness in rank
Togetherness in rank is defined by equal distance from the genus. Species are together in rank if they are equally near or far from the genus.
E.g., 'horse' and 'cow' are both equally close to 'animal'.
4
Apply the distinction to an example
Take two species under the same genus, like 'human' and 'angel'. Are they together in rank? Yes, if they are equally close to the genus 'substance' (in a broad sense). But are they together in species? No, because they are different species.
This clarifies the two dimensions of togetherness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الزمان ومعا في الطبع : فهما إما أن يكونا مثلا وبين تكافؤ الوجود كما في الأمور الإضافية، وإما أن يكونا منافيين كما في الأنواع تحت جنس واحد ليس لا بهما معا في الطبع فقط بل في المرتبة كما مرَّ . فالمعية في الأنواع معية بإزاء التقدم والتأخر . وأما معيتها في المرتبة فلأنها متساوية في القرب والبعد من المبدأ الذي هو الجنس؛ ذلك أن يعلم من هذا حال معا في الشرف. وأما معا في العلية فذلك مشكل. والكلام فيه في الحركة كما هو حقه يعرف من العلم الطبيعي؛ وإنها أي الحركة ومقولة أن ينفعل سواء. ثم من الحركة ما يكون في الجوهر مثل كون النطفة إنسانًا، ومنها ما يكون في الكيف وهو الاستحالة مثل التسود والتسخن، ومنها ما يكون في الكم وإنه إما إلى الزيادة، وذلك إن كان بالزائد فهو النمو، وإن كان بانبساط الجوهر فهو التخلخل . وإما إلى النقصان، وذلك إن كان بتخلخل الأجزاء فهو الذبول، وإن كان لا بالتخلل فهو التكاثف ومنها ما يكون في المكان فهو النقلة . وعندي أن الحركة نوع آخر وهو الحركة في الوضع كحركة الجسم على مركز نفسه مستديرا؛ فإن هذه الحركة لا تكون مكانية. وربما تكون لا في مكان، وإن كانت في مكان لم يفارق المتمكن مكانه بالكلية بل يكون مستبدلا للوضع دون الأين. وبالجملة فالمتقدم والمتأخر والمقابل والمع والحركة كلها من الألفاظ المستعملة في تعليم المقولات. وأما النظر في حال الاتفاق والتواطؤ وما على موضوع وما في موضوع وغير ذلك فكان محتاجًا تقديمه على المقولات؛ إذ لا بد من استعمالها في تعليم المقولات؛ فاعتبر بما عرفت فإن في هذا القدر كفاية في هذا الباب، والله أعلم بالصواب وإليه المرجع والمآب.
In time and together in nature: they are either similar and between equivalence of existence as in relational matters, or they are opposed as in species under one genus, not being together in nature only but in rank as has passed. So togetherness in species is togetherness in relation to precedence and posteriority. As for their togetherness in rank, it is because they are equal in nearness and distance from the principle which is the genus; from this one knows the state of togetherness in honor. As for togetherness in causality, that is problematic. Speech about it is in motion as is its right, known from natural science; and it (motion) and the category of being affected are the same. Then of motion, some is in substance like a sperm becoming a human; some is in quality, which is transformation, like blackening and heating; some is in quantity, and it is either toward increase, and if by addition it is growth, and if by expansion of substance it is rarefaction; or toward decrease, and if by rarefaction of parts it is withering, and if not by rarefaction it is condensation. And some is in place, which is translocation. And according to me, there is another type, which is motion in posture, like the motion of a body around its own center rotating; for this motion is not locative. It might be not in place, and if it is in place, the occupant does not leave its place entirely but rather changes posture without changing location. In summary, priority, posteriority, opposition, togetherness, and motion are all terms used in teaching the categories. As for the consideration of the states of agreement and univocity, and 'on a subject' and 'in a subject' and other things, it would have been necessary to present them before the categories, since they must be used in teaching the categories. So consider what you have known, for this amount suffices for this chapter, and God knows best what is correct, and to Him is the return and the final abode.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a concluding section on the categories, specifically discussing the concepts of 'togetherness' (ma'iyya) and motion (haraka). The author first addresses two things being together in time and nature: they can be identical in existence like correlatives, or opposed like species under one genus. Togetherness in species is relative to priority, while togetherness in rank is based on equal distance from the genus. Then the author turns to motion, which is known from natural science. Motion is classified into five types: in substance (e.g., sperm becoming human), in quality (transformation like blackening), in quantity (increase or decrease), and in place (translocation). The author adds a sixth type: motion in posture (wad'), such as rotation around a center, which is not locative. Finally, the author notes that terms like priority, posteriority, opposition, togetherness, and motion are used in teaching the categories, and hints that a prior discussion of other logical terms (agreement, univocity, etc.) would have been beneficial.

Six Types of Motion: Substance, Quality, Quantity, Place, and Posture
Classifies motion into six types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six types of motion according to the author, including his addition of motion in posture.

Core Idea
Motion is not limited to four categories; the author adds posture as a sixth type.

In this part of the passage, the author classifies motion into six types: in substance, quality, quantity, place, and his addition: posture. He emphasizes that motion in posture, like rotation, is not locative and can occur without changing place. Understanding these types is essential for categorizing change in the physical world of Islamic philosophy and natural science.

Guided Path
1
List the types of motion explicitly mentioned
Read from 'min al-haraka ma yakunu...' to '...al-nuqla'. The author enumerates motion in substance, quality, quantity, and place.
The standard four from Aristotle.
2
Identify the author's original contribution
After stating the usual types, the author says 'And according to me, there is another type, which is motion in posture (al-wad').' This is his addition.
Motion in posture includes rotation around a center without change of place.
3
Understand why posture is distinct from place
The author explains that such motion is not locative; it may occur in place but the object does not leave its place entirely, only changes orientation.
E.g., a spinning top.
4
Apply the classification to a new example
Given a physical change, determine which type of motion it falls under: e.g., freezing water is motion in quality (transformation), a seed growing is motion in quantity (increase).

Binary vs Ternary Propositions: The Need for an Explicit Copula
Author discusses when an explicit copula is necessary in binary and ternary propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a categorical proposition requires an explicit copula by analyzing the type of predicate.

Core Idea
The copula is only needed when the predicate does not inherently indicate the relation.

In this passage, the author explains that not all propositions need an explicit copula. When the predicate is a verb or a derived noun, the relation to the subject is already implied. We will learn to identify these cases and understand the objection raised about specificity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the predicate type
Is the predicate a verb (kalima), a derived noun (ism mushtaqq), or a simple noun? This determines whether the relation is inherently indicated.
For example, 'Zayd strikes' has a verb predicate; 'Zayd is just' has a noun predicate.
2
Check for inherent relation
Verbs and derived nouns inherently indicate a relation to the subject; simple nouns do not. The verb signifies the action and its connection to the subject.
Derived nouns like 'striker' also indicate the relation but lack temporal specificity.
3
Determine if explicit copula is needed
If the predicate inherently indicates the relation, no explicit copula is required; otherwise, one is needed to link the predicate to the subject.
In 'Zayd strikes', the verb itself shows the relation; in 'Zayd is just', an explicit 'is' or 'exists' is required.
4
Consider the objection about specificity
An objector argues that verbs do not indicate the subject specifically. The author responds that the verb implicitly contains the copula, making an explicit copula unnecessary in speech.
This shows that the copula in Arabic is a tool resembling a verb, not a noun.

Contrary to the binary [proposition]. For the explicit mention of that particle may be necessary or may not be, as when the predicate is a verb (kalimat) or what runs in the course of verbs, such as derived nouns. For the verb by its very nature is connected to the subject—how could it not be?—and it signifies a meaning belonging to the subject in a signification that is implicit in it. Likewise, what is from derived nouns like 'the beater' (al-dārib) and similar, but the verb signifies what is common between them and also a specific time, unlike the derived noun. And if that is the case, then we have no need to explicitly mention that particle, namely the copula (al-rābiṭa). For the copula is needed only to indicate the relation (nisba) of the predicate to the subject when the predicate is a noun in itself. But when it is not [a noun], there is no need for it, and that indication is already present in our saying 'Zayd strikes' (Zayd yaḍribu) or 'a striker' (ḍāribun) likewise. Then someone might say at this point: The verb, even if it indicates the subject, does not indicate the specific [subject]; and the need is only for what binds to the specific subject in a binding that points to it. For if you say 'Zayd such-and-such,' then 'he' (huwa) refers back to Zayd and pertains to him only. But if you say 'Zayd will be such-and-such,' it is not so, because your saying 'will be' (yakūnu) does not indicate him specifically. But we say: The meaning of our statement that the copula is not needed when the predicate is a verb or what runs its course is that it is not needed explicitly. For this reason it is said that the verb contains the copula implicitly, so that the meaning of our saying 'Zayd will be such-and-such' is 'Zayd will be he such-and-such.' So the copula is a tool resembling the verb in the language of the Arabs, not a tool resembling the noun. If you say: The verb is either such that it indicates the relation of the predicate to the subject, in which case the copula is not needed at all, or it does not indicate it, in which case it is needed, as with other [predicates]. We say: Even if it indicates it, it does not indicate it specifically, as was mentioned, so it indicates it in one respect but not in another, so it is needed in one respect but not in another. Then even if it does not indicate it specifically in itself, it is not devoid of indicating it specifically in our saying 'Zayd will be such-and-such' because the basis of following something is that it pertains to the other thing. If that is the case, then it is not needed explicitly. So propositions, with respect to what we have mentioned, are on three levels: a level in which the relation is indicated explicitly, a level in which the relation is indicated but not explicitly, and a level in which the relation is not indicated at all. The last is from the perfect binary propositions just as the first is from the perfect ternary propositions, unlike the second. Then the ternary propositions are those in which the copula is explicitly mentioned, such as our saying 'The human being exists just' (al-insānu yūjadu ʿadlan) or 'he is just' (huwa ʿadlun). The word 'exists' (yūjadu) and the word 'he' (huwa) are not included as part of the predicate themselves, but rather to indicate that the predicate exists for the subject. As for the word 'exists,' it indicates the existence of the predicate for the subject in the future; as for the word 'he,' it indicates the existence of the predicate for the subject absolutely. So the copula indicates the relation of the predicate, and the quantifier (al-sūr) indicates the quantity of the subject. For this reason, the copula is variable on the side of the predicate, and the quantifier is variable on the side of the subject. So if the particle of negation is attached to the ternary [proposition], as in our saying 'Zayd is not just' (Zaydun laysa yūjadu ʿādilan), that particle enters upon the copula and negates its binding. And if it is attached as in our saying 'Zayd exists not-just' (Zaydun yūjadu lā ʿādilan), then the copula enters upon the particle of negation, so the particle of negation becomes part of the predicate. Thus 'just' alone is not the predicate, but rather the predicate is 'not-just' (al-lā ʿādil) affirmatively, contrary to the former. So it is as if he is described as 'unjust' (ghayru ʿādilin) so that its negation by the particle of negation becomes correct. Thus one says 'Zayd does not exist not-just' (Zaydun laysa yūjadu lā ʿādilan) or 'unjust,' and here two affirmatives or two negatives are posited. For our saying 'exists just' is opposed to our saying 'Zayd does not exist just'; these are the simple affirmative and simple negative. And our saying 'Zayd exists not-just' is opposed to our saying 'Zayd does not exist not-just'; these are the modified affirmative and modified negative. For the proposition that
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the copula (al-rābiṭa) in categorical propositions, primarily addressing when an explicit copula is needed and when it is not. The author begins by contrasting binary propositions (thunāʾiyya) with ternary propositions (thulāthiyya). In binary propositions, the predicate is a verb (kalima) or a derived noun (ism mushtaqq), such as 'strikes' or 'striker'. The verb inherently indicates the relation (nisba) to the subject, as seen in 'Zayd strikes' (Zayd yaḍribu) where the verb form itself shows the connection. Similarly, a derived noun like 'striker' (ḍārib) also implies the relation but without the temporal specificity that the verb provides. Therefore, when the predicate is a verb or similar, no explicit copula like 'exists' or 'is' is required because the predicate itself carries the relational meaning. 

An objector then raises a point: although the verb indicates the subject, it does not specify which subject (e.g., 'yadribu' does not by itself single out Zayd). The need is for a term that binds the predicate specifically to the subject. For instance, saying 'Zayd such-and-such' (Zayd kadha) with the pronoun 'he' (huwa) as copula clearly refers back to Zayd alone. But 'Zayd will be such-and-such' (Zayd yakūnu kadha) uses 'becomes' (yakūnu) which does not specifically point to Zayd. The author responds that the copula is not needed explicitly (bil-taṣrīḥ) when the predicate is a verb because the verb implicitly contains the copula. In 'Zayd yakūnu kadha', the implied meaning is 'Zayd yakūnu huwa kadha' (Zayd will be he such-and-such). Thus, the copula in Arabic is a tool resembling a verb (kalima), not a noun. 

Further objection: if the verb indicates the relation, then no copula is needed at all; if it does not, then it is needed as with any noun predicate. The author refines this: the verb indicates the relation but not specifically (muʿayyanan), so it indicates it in one respect and not in another. Hence the copula is needed in one respect (to specify the binding) but not in another (to indicate the relation itself). However, even if the verb by itself does not indicate the relation specifically, in actual usage 'Zayd yakūnu kadha' does indicate it specifically because the natural flow of speech connects the verb to the subject. Therefore, the explicit copula is ultimately not required.

This leads to a threefold classification of propositions regarding the manifestation of the relation (nisba): (1) those where the relation is indicated explicitly (bi-ʿaynihā) – these are perfect ternary propositions (thulāthiyyāt tāmma) with an explicit copula; (2) those where the relation is indicated but not explicitly – these are imperfect ternary propositions; (3) those where the relation is not indicated at all – these are perfect binary propositions (thunāʾiyyāt tāmma). The first type includes sentences like 'The human exists just' (al-insānu yūjadu ʿadlan) or 'he is just' (huwa ʿadlun). The words 'exists' (yūjadu) and 'he' (huwa) function as the copula, not as part of the predicate. They indicate the existence of the predicate for the subject. 'Yūjadu' specifies future existence, while 'huwa' indicates absolute existence. The copula thus signifies the relation of the predicate; the quantifier (al-sūr) signifies the quantity of the subject. The copula varies according to the predicate (tense etc.), while the quantifier varies with the subject.

Finally, the author discusses the interaction of negation (ḥarf al-salb) with the ternary proposition. If the negation is attached to the copula, as in 'Zayd is not just' (Zaydun laysa yūjadu ʿādilan), the negation negates the copula's binding, producing a simple negative. If the negation is placed before the predicate, as in 'Zayd exists not-just' (Zaydun yūjadu lā ʿādilan), the negation becomes part of the predicate, yielding a modified predicate (maḥmūl maʿdūl). Here 'not-just' (lā ʿādil) is the predicate, and the proposition is affirmative with a modified predicate. To negate such a proposition, one would say 'Zayd does not exist not-just' (Zaydun laysa yūjadu lā ʿādilan) or 'unjust' (ghayru ʿādil). This yields two sets of opposites: the simple affirmative (Zayd exists just) vs. simple negative (Zayd does not exist just), and the modified affirmative (Zayd exists not-just) vs. modified negative (Zayd does not exist not-just). The passage concludes by introducing these as the two types of simple and modified propositions.

An explicit copula is only necessary when the predicate is a simple noun that does not inherently indicate the relation; verbs and derived nouns carry the relation implicitly.

Threefold Classification of Propositions by Relational Indication
Author classifies propositions into three levels regarding the indication of the relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any categorical proposition into one of three levels based on how the relation (nisba) is indicated: explicitly, implicitly, or not at all.

Core Idea
Propositions differ in how explicitly the relation between subject and predicate is expressed.

Building on the discussion of the copula, the author introduces a tripartite classification of propositions. This classification depends on whether the relation of the predicate to the subject is indicated explicitly, implicitly, or not at all. We will learn to identify each level and understand its significance in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the presence of a copula
Check if the proposition includes an explicit copula like 'exists' (yūjadu) or 'he' (huwa) that links the predicate to the subject.
If yes, the relation is indicated explicitly.
2
Check for implicit relation
If there is no explicit copula, see if the predicate itself (e.g., a verb) implies the relation to the subject.
Example: 'Zayd strikes' – the verb 'strikes' indicates the relation, but not explicitly via a copula.
3
Determine if there is no relation at all
If there is neither an explicit copula nor an implicit indication, the proposition does not express any relation; such are perfect binary propositions.
The passage states this is a level where the relation is not indicated at all.
4
Match to the three levels
Level 1: Perfect ternary (explicit relation). Level 2: Indicated but not explicitly (implicit). Level 3: Perfect binary (no relation indicated).
The second level is neither perfect binary nor perfect ternary; it includes propositions with verb predicates.

Contrary to the binary [proposition]. For the explicit mention of that particle may be necessary or may not be, as when the predicate is a verb (kalimat) or what runs in the course of verbs, such as derived nouns. For the verb by its very nature is connected to the subject—how could it not be?—and it signifies a meaning belonging to the subject in a signification that is implicit in it. Likewise, what is from derived nouns like 'the beater' (al-dārib) and similar, but the verb signifies what is common between them and also a specific time, unlike the derived noun. And if that is the case, then we have no need to explicitly mention that particle, namely the copula (al-rābiṭa). For the copula is needed only to indicate the relation (nisba) of the predicate to the subject when the predicate is a noun in itself. But when it is not [a noun], there is no need for it, and that indication is already present in our saying 'Zayd strikes' (Zayd yaḍribu) or 'a striker' (ḍāribun) likewise. Then someone might say at this point: The verb, even if it indicates the subject, does not indicate the specific [subject]; and the need is only for what binds to the specific subject in a binding that points to it. For if you say 'Zayd such-and-such,' then 'he' (huwa) refers back to Zayd and pertains to him only. But if you say 'Zayd will be such-and-such,' it is not so, because your saying 'will be' (yakūnu) does not indicate him specifically. But we say: The meaning of our statement that the copula is not needed when the predicate is a verb or what runs its course is that it is not needed explicitly. For this reason it is said that the verb contains the copula implicitly, so that the meaning of our saying 'Zayd will be such-and-such' is 'Zayd will be he such-and-such.' So the copula is a tool resembling the verb in the language of the Arabs, not a tool resembling the noun. If you say: The verb is either such that it indicates the relation of the predicate to the subject, in which case the copula is not needed at all, or it does not indicate it, in which case it is needed, as with other [predicates]. We say: Even if it indicates it, it does not indicate it specifically, as was mentioned, so it indicates it in one respect but not in another, so it is needed in one respect but not in another. Then even if it does not indicate it specifically in itself, it is not devoid of indicating it specifically in our saying 'Zayd will be such-and-such' because the basis of following something is that it pertains to the other thing. If that is the case, then it is not needed explicitly. So propositions, with respect to what we have mentioned, are on three levels: a level in which the relation is indicated explicitly, a level in which the relation is indicated but not explicitly, and a level in which the relation is not indicated at all. The last is from the perfect binary propositions just as the first is from the perfect ternary propositions, unlike the second. Then the ternary propositions are those in which the copula is explicitly mentioned, such as our saying 'The human being exists just' (al-insānu yūjadu ʿadlan) or 'he is just' (huwa ʿadlun). The word 'exists' (yūjadu) and the word 'he' (huwa) are not included as part of the predicate themselves, but rather to indicate that the predicate exists for the subject. As for the word 'exists,' it indicates the existence of the predicate for the subject in the future; as for the word 'he,' it indicates the existence of the predicate for the subject absolutely. So the copula indicates the relation of the predicate, and the quantifier (al-sūr) indicates the quantity of the subject. For this reason, the copula is variable on the side of the predicate, and the quantifier is variable on the side of the subject. So if the particle of negation is attached to the ternary [proposition], as in our saying 'Zayd is not just' (Zaydun laysa yūjadu ʿādilan), that particle enters upon the copula and negates its binding. And if it is attached as in our saying 'Zayd exists not-just' (Zaydun yūjadu lā ʿādilan), then the copula enters upon the particle of negation, so the particle of negation becomes part of the predicate. Thus 'just' alone is not the predicate, but rather the predicate is 'not-just' (al-lā ʿādil) affirmatively, contrary to the former. So it is as if he is described as 'unjust' (ghayru ʿādilin) so that its negation by the particle of negation becomes correct. Thus one says 'Zayd does not exist not-just' (Zaydun laysa yūjadu lā ʿādilan) or 'unjust,' and here two affirmatives or two negatives are posited. For our saying 'exists just' is opposed to our saying 'Zayd does not exist just'; these are the simple affirmative and simple negative. And our saying 'Zayd exists not-just' is opposed to our saying 'Zayd does not exist not-just'; these are the modified affirmative and modified negative. For the proposition that

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the copula (al-rābiṭa) in categorical propositions, primarily addressing when an explicit copula is needed and when it is not. The author begins by contrasting binary propositions (thunāʾiyya) with ternary propositions (thulāthiyya). In binary propositions, the predicate is a verb (kalima) or a derived noun (ism mushtaqq), such as 'strikes' or 'striker'. The verb inherently indicates the relation (nisba) to the subject, as seen in 'Zayd strikes' (Zayd yaḍribu) where the verb form itself shows the connection. Similarly, a derived noun like 'striker' (ḍārib) also implies the relation but without the temporal specificity that the verb provides. Therefore, when the predicate is a verb or similar, no explicit copula like 'exists' or 'is' is required because the predicate itself carries the relational meaning. 

An objector then raises a point: although the verb indicates the subject, it does not specify which subject (e.g., 'yadribu' does not by itself single out Zayd). The need is for a term that binds the predicate specifically to the subject. For instance, saying 'Zayd such-and-such' (Zayd kadha) with the pronoun 'he' (huwa) as copula clearly refers back to Zayd alone. But 'Zayd will be such-and-such' (Zayd yakūnu kadha) uses 'becomes' (yakūnu) which does not specifically point to Zayd. The author responds that the copula is not needed explicitly (bil-taṣrīḥ) when the predicate is a verb because the verb implicitly contains the copula. In 'Zayd yakūnu kadha', the implied meaning is 'Zayd yakūnu huwa kadha' (Zayd will be he such-and-such). Thus, the copula in Arabic is a tool resembling a verb (kalima), not a noun. 

Further objection: if the verb indicates the relation, then no copula is needed at all; if it does not, then it is needed as with any noun predicate. The author refines this: the verb indicates the relation but not specifically (muʿayyanan), so it indicates it in one respect and not in another. Hence the copula is needed in one respect (to specify the binding) but not in another (to indicate the relation itself). However, even if the verb by itself does not indicate the relation specifically, in actual usage 'Zayd yakūnu kadha' does indicate it specifically because the natural flow of speech connects the verb to the subject. Therefore, the explicit copula is ultimately not required.

This leads to a threefold classification of propositions regarding the manifestation of the relation (nisba): (1) those where the relation is indicated explicitly (bi-ʿaynihā) – these are perfect ternary propositions (thulāthiyyāt tāmma) with an explicit copula; (2) those where the relation is indicated but not explicitly – these are imperfect ternary propositions; (3) those where the relation is not indicated at all – these are perfect binary propositions (thunāʾiyyāt tāmma). The first type includes sentences like 'The human exists just' (al-insānu yūjadu ʿadlan) or 'he is just' (huwa ʿadlun). The words 'exists' (yūjadu) and 'he' (huwa) function as the copula, not as part of the predicate. They indicate the existence of the predicate for the subject. 'Yūjadu' specifies future existence, while 'huwa' indicates absolute existence. The copula thus signifies the relation of the predicate; the quantifier (al-sūr) signifies the quantity of the subject. The copula varies according to the predicate (tense etc.), while the quantifier varies with the subject.

Finally, the author discusses the interaction of negation (ḥarf al-salb) with the ternary proposition. If the negation is attached to the copula, as in 'Zayd is not just' (Zaydun laysa yūjadu ʿādilan), the negation negates the copula's binding, producing a simple negative. If the negation is placed before the predicate, as in 'Zayd exists not-just' (Zaydun yūjadu lā ʿādilan), the negation becomes part of the predicate, yielding a modified predicate (maḥmūl maʿdūl). Here 'not-just' (lā ʿādil) is the predicate, and the proposition is affirmative with a modified predicate. To negate such a proposition, one would say 'Zayd does not exist not-just' (Zaydun laysa yūjadu lā ʿādilan) or 'unjust' (ghayru ʿādil). This yields two sets of opposites: the simple affirmative (Zayd exists just) vs. simple negative (Zayd does not exist just), and the modified affirmative (Zayd exists not-just) vs. modified negative (Zayd does not exist not-just). The passage concludes by introducing these as the two types of simple and modified propositions.

The Copula and Quantifier in Ternary Propositions
Author explains the copula and quantifier in ternary propositions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the copula and quantifier in a ternary proposition and explain their distinct functions.

Core Idea
In ternary propositions, the copula indicates the relation of the predicate, while the quantifier indicates the quantity of the subject.

Having established that ternary propositions require an explicit copula, the author now examines two common copula forms: 'exists' (yūjadu) and 'he' (huwa). He also distinguishes the copula from the quantifier (al-sūr), which specifies the quantity of the subject. This lesson will help you parse the components of a ternary proposition correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the copula in a ternary proposition
Look for words like 'exists' (yūjadu) or 'he' (huwa) that link the predicate to the subject. These are not part of the predicate but serve as the copula.
Example: 'The human exists just' – 'exists' is the copula.
2
Distinguish the types of copula
The copula 'exists' (yūjadu) indicates existence in the future, while 'he' (huwa) indicates existence absolutely, without temporal specification.
Both indicate that the predicate holds for the subject.
3
Locate the quantifier (al-sūr)
The quantifier indicates the quantity of the subject (e.g., 'every', 'some'). It is attached to the subject side, while the copula is on the predicate side.
Example: 'Every human is just' – 'every' is the quantifier.
4
Understand their variation
The copula varies according to the predicate (e.g., tense, negation), and the quantifier varies according to the subject (e.g., universality, particularity).
This shows they are independent components.

المحمول للموضوع مطلقًا: فالرابطة تدل على نسبة المحمول، والسور يدل على كمية الموضوع. ولهذا ما كانت الرابطة متعددة في جانب المحمول، والسور متعددة في جانب في جانب الموضوع. فلو قرن بها أي بالثلاثية حرف السلب كما في قولنا زيد ليس يوجد عادلًا كان ذلك الحرف يدخل على الرابطة فيسلب ربطها. ولو قرن بها كما في قولنا زيد يوجد لا عادلا كانت الرابطة تدخل على حرف السلب فيكون حرف السلب جزءا من المحمول: فلم يكن العادل» بانفراده محمولا بل حمله «اللا عادل» بالإيجاب على خلاف ذلك. فكأنه وصف بأنه غير عادل حتى يصح سلبه بحرف السلب كذلك فيقال زيد ليس يوجد لا عادلًا أو غير عادل فيفرض هنا موجبتان أو سالبتان. فإن قولنا يوجد عادلًا يقابل قولنا زيد ليس يوجد عادلا؛ وهما الموجبة والسالبة البسيطتان. وقولنا زيد يوجد لا عادلًا يقابل قولنا زيد ليس يوجد لا عادلا؛ وهما الموجبة والسالبة المعدولتان. فإن القضية التي
Contrary to the binary [proposition]. For the explicit mention of that particle may be necessary or may not be, as when the predicate is a verb (kalimat) or what runs in the course of verbs, such as derived nouns. For the verb by its very nature is connected to the subject—how could it not be?—and it signifies a meaning belonging to the subject in a signification that is implicit in it. Likewise, what is from derived nouns like 'the beater' (al-dārib) and similar, but the verb signifies what is common between them and also a specific time, unlike the derived noun. And if that is the case, then we have no need to explicitly mention that particle, namely the copula (al-rābiṭa). For the copula is needed only to indicate the relation (nisba) of the predicate to the subject when the predicate is a noun in itself. But when it is not [a noun], there is no need for it, and that indication is already present in our saying 'Zayd strikes' (Zayd yaḍribu) or 'a striker' (ḍāribun) likewise. Then someone might say at this point: The verb, even if it indicates the subject, does not indicate the specific [subject]; and the need is only for what binds to the specific subject in a binding that points to it. For if you say 'Zayd such-and-such,' then 'he' (huwa) refers back to Zayd and pertains to him only. But if you say 'Zayd will be such-and-such,' it is not so, because your saying 'will be' (yakūnu) does not indicate him specifically. But we say: The meaning of our statement that the copula is not needed when the predicate is a verb or what runs its course is that it is not needed explicitly. For this reason it is said that the verb contains the copula implicitly, so that the meaning of our saying 'Zayd will be such-and-such' is 'Zayd will be he such-and-such.' So the copula is a tool resembling the verb in the language of the Arabs, not a tool resembling the noun. If you say: The verb is either such that it indicates the relation of the predicate to the subject, in which case the copula is not needed at all, or it does not indicate it, in which case it is needed, as with other [predicates]. We say: Even if it indicates it, it does not indicate it specifically, as was mentioned, so it indicates it in one respect but not in another, so it is needed in one respect but not in another. Then even if it does not indicate it specifically in itself, it is not devoid of indicating it specifically in our saying 'Zayd will be such-and-such' because the basis of following something is that it pertains to the other thing. If that is the case, then it is not needed explicitly. So propositions, with respect to what we have mentioned, are on three levels: a level in which the relation is indicated explicitly, a level in which the relation is indicated but not explicitly, and a level in which the relation is not indicated at all. The last is from the perfect binary propositions just as the first is from the perfect ternary propositions, unlike the second. Then the ternary propositions are those in which the copula is explicitly mentioned, such as our saying 'The human being exists just' (al-insānu yūjadu ʿadlan) or 'he is just' (huwa ʿadlun). The word 'exists' (yūjadu) and the word 'he' (huwa) are not included as part of the predicate themselves, but rather to indicate that the predicate exists for the subject. As for the word 'exists,' it indicates the existence of the predicate for the subject in the future; as for the word 'he,' it indicates the existence of the predicate for the subject absolutely. So the copula indicates the relation of the predicate, and the quantifier (al-sūr) indicates the quantity of the subject. For this reason, the copula is variable on the side of the predicate, and the quantifier is variable on the side of the subject. So if the particle of negation is attached to the ternary [proposition], as in our saying 'Zayd is not just' (Zaydun laysa yūjadu ʿādilan), that particle enters upon the copula and negates its binding. And if it is attached as in our saying 'Zayd exists not-just' (Zaydun yūjadu lā ʿādilan), then the copula enters upon the particle of negation, so the particle of negation becomes part of the predicate. Thus 'just' alone is not the predicate, but rather the predicate is 'not-just' (al-lā ʿādil) affirmatively, contrary to the former. So it is as if he is described as 'unjust' (ghayru ʿādilin) so that its negation by the particle of negation becomes correct. Thus one says 'Zayd does not exist not-just' (Zaydun laysa yūjadu lā ʿādilan) or 'unjust,' and here two affirmatives or two negatives are posited. For our saying 'exists just' is opposed to our saying 'Zayd does not exist just'; these are the simple affirmative and simple negative. And our saying 'Zayd exists not-just' is opposed to our saying 'Zayd does not exist not-just'; these are the modified affirmative and modified negative. For the proposition that

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the copula (al-rābiṭa) in categorical propositions, primarily addressing when an explicit copula is needed and when it is not. The author begins by contrasting binary propositions (thunāʾiyya) with ternary propositions (thulāthiyya). In binary propositions, the predicate is a verb (kalima) or a derived noun (ism mushtaqq), such as 'strikes' or 'striker'. The verb inherently indicates the relation (nisba) to the subject, as seen in 'Zayd strikes' (Zayd yaḍribu) where the verb form itself shows the connection. Similarly, a derived noun like 'striker' (ḍārib) also implies the relation but without the temporal specificity that the verb provides. Therefore, when the predicate is a verb or similar, no explicit copula like 'exists' or 'is' is required because the predicate itself carries the relational meaning. 

An objector then raises a point: although the verb indicates the subject, it does not specify which subject (e.g., 'yadribu' does not by itself single out Zayd). The need is for a term that binds the predicate specifically to the subject. For instance, saying 'Zayd such-and-such' (Zayd kadha) with the pronoun 'he' (huwa) as copula clearly refers back to Zayd alone. But 'Zayd will be such-and-such' (Zayd yakūnu kadha) uses 'becomes' (yakūnu) which does not specifically point to Zayd. The author responds that the copula is not needed explicitly (bil-taṣrīḥ) when the predicate is a verb because the verb implicitly contains the copula. In 'Zayd yakūnu kadha', the implied meaning is 'Zayd yakūnu huwa kadha' (Zayd will be he such-and-such). Thus, the copula in Arabic is a tool resembling a verb (kalima), not a noun. 

Further objection: if the verb indicates the relation, then no copula is needed at all; if it does not, then it is needed as with any noun predicate. The author refines this: the verb indicates the relation but not specifically (muʿayyanan), so it indicates it in one respect and not in another. Hence the copula is needed in one respect (to specify the binding) but not in another (to indicate the relation itself). However, even if the verb by itself does not indicate the relation specifically, in actual usage 'Zayd yakūnu kadha' does indicate it specifically because the natural flow of speech connects the verb to the subject. Therefore, the explicit copula is ultimately not required.

This leads to a threefold classification of propositions regarding the manifestation of the relation (nisba): (1) those where the relation is indicated explicitly (bi-ʿaynihā) – these are perfect ternary propositions (thulāthiyyāt tāmma) with an explicit copula; (2) those where the relation is indicated but not explicitly – these are imperfect ternary propositions; (3) those where the relation is not indicated at all – these are perfect binary propositions (thunāʾiyyāt tāmma). The first type includes sentences like 'The human exists just' (al-insānu yūjadu ʿadlan) or 'he is just' (huwa ʿadlun). The words 'exists' (yūjadu) and 'he' (huwa) function as the copula, not as part of the predicate. They indicate the existence of the predicate for the subject. 'Yūjadu' specifies future existence, while 'huwa' indicates absolute existence. The copula thus signifies the relation of the predicate; the quantifier (al-sūr) signifies the quantity of the subject. The copula varies according to the predicate (tense etc.), while the quantifier varies with the subject.

Finally, the author discusses the interaction of negation (ḥarf al-salb) with the ternary proposition. If the negation is attached to the copula, as in 'Zayd is not just' (Zaydun laysa yūjadu ʿādilan), the negation negates the copula's binding, producing a simple negative. If the negation is placed before the predicate, as in 'Zayd exists not-just' (Zaydun yūjadu lā ʿādilan), the negation becomes part of the predicate, yielding a modified predicate (maḥmūl maʿdūl). Here 'not-just' (lā ʿādil) is the predicate, and the proposition is affirmative with a modified predicate. To negate such a proposition, one would say 'Zayd does not exist not-just' (Zaydun laysa yūjadu lā ʿādilan) or 'unjust' (ghayru ʿādil). This yields two sets of opposites: the simple affirmative (Zayd exists just) vs. simple negative (Zayd does not exist just), and the modified affirmative (Zayd exists not-just) vs. modified negative (Zayd does not exist not-just). The passage concludes by introducing these as the two types of simple and modified propositions.

Checkpoint
What does the copula indicate in a ternary proposition?
A) The quantity of the subject
B) The relation of the predicate to the subject
C) The time of the action
D) The meaning of the predicate itself

Key Takeaway
In ternary propositions, the copula indicates the relation of the predicate, while the quantifier indicates the quantity of the subject; they serve distinct logical functions.

Simple and Modified Propositions: The Role of Negation
Author explains the effect of negation placement in ternary propositions, leading to simple and modified propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish simple propositions from modified propositions (maʿdūla) and explain how negation interacts with the copula.

Core Idea
The position of the negation particle determines whether a proposition is simple or modified, affecting both meaning and logical behavior.

The author now examines how negation interacts with the copula in ternary propositions. When the negation particle precedes the copula, it negates the relation, producing a simple negative proposition. When it follows the copula and attaches to the predicate, it becomes part of a modified predicate, creating a modified affirmative proposition. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing propositional opposites.

Guided Path
1
Identify the position of the negation particle
In a ternary proposition, locate the negation particle (e.g., 'laysa' or 'lā'). Is it placed before or after the copula?
Example: 'Zayd is not just' vs. 'Zayd exists not-just'.
2
Determine if negation is on the copula or predicate
If negation precedes the copula, it negates the binding (simple negative). If it follows the copula and modifies the predicate, the negation becomes part of a modified predicate (maḥmūl maʿdūl).
In 'Zayd exists not-just', the predicate is 'not-just', not 'just'.
3
Classify as simple or modified
Simple propositions have an unmodified predicate; modified propositions have a predicate that includes a negation particle. Both can be affirmative or negative.
For example: 'Zayd exists not-just' is a modified affirmative; 'Zayd does not exist not-just' is a modified negative.
4
Identify the opposites
The simple affirmative 'Zayd exists just' is opposed to the simple negative 'Zayd does not exist just'. The modified affirmative 'Zayd exists not-just' is opposed to the modified negative 'Zayd does not exist not-just'.
This shows two distinct pairs of contradictory propositions.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the copula (al-rābiṭa) in categorical propositions, primarily addressing when an explicit copula is needed and when it is not.
